4. September 2016
Supervisors 
Professor Peter Glarborg  
Assistant Professor Hao Wu

Page 1 of 111 
 
Abstract  
Biomass is considered to be an important renewable energy source to reduce the world’s dependence on 
fossil fuels. Gasification, which converts solid biomass into a combustible gas, is regarded to be one of the 
most promising technologies for the utilization of biomass. The produced gas can be burned in engines, 
turbines or fuel cells to generate electricity and heat however the gas may also be used as a feedstock to 
synthesise liquid fuels and commodity chemicals. Gasification does thereby increase the opportunities of 
using biomass as an energy source compared with combustion. 
The produced gas contains several contaminations, which have to be removed before the gas can be utilized. 
Limitations in current hot gas cleaning methods for chlorine species may be a major stumbling block for end 
use as engines, turbines and fuel cells all require extremely low levels (>1 ppm) of these contaminants. 
During pyrolysis, chlorine is typically release as hydrogen chloride and methyl chloride. Hydrogen chloride 
is the desired product as it can easily be removed however cleaning for methyl chloride is more challenging 
as the thermal conversion of methyl chloride under gasification is currently not well understood. 
A detailed chemical kinetic model for methyl chloride was developed during this project to characterize the 
transformation of methyl chloride under gasification conditions. The model were evaluated against various 
experimental data from literature such as flow reactor, iginition delay and flame speed data. For the flow 
reactor experiments, the model gave satisfactory predictions of the methyl chloride and the product species. 
For the iginition and flame speed data the model slightly under predicted the experimental data. As the 
model have shown good agreement with the experimental data over a wide range of conditions, it can be 
used to model the thermal conversion of methyl chloride under gasification conditions with reasonable 
accuracy. Caution should however be taken when using the model as the gas composition in the experimental 
data varies significantly from gasification gas compositions. 
The chemical kinetic model was used to model the thermal conversion of methyl chloride within different 
gasification technologies (Updraft, downdraft, fluidized bed and entrained bed gasifers). In the downdraft 
and entrained bed gasifier methyl chloride will be fully converted into hydrogen chloride due to the high 
temperature within these gasifiers. For low temperature fluidized bed (650-700 °C) and updraft gasifiers, it 
was found that all the methyl chloride formed within the gasifier will appear in the exit gas. For fluidized bed 
gasifiers operating at typical gasification temperatures only a fraction of the methyl chloride formed within 
the gasifier is decomposed into hydrogen chloride. Thus, methyl chloride will be present in the gas produced 
from fluidzed bed and updraft gasifers. The conversion of methyl chloride within fluidized bed gasifiers can 
be significantly improved by injecting secondary air. By doing so all methyl chloride from within the gasifer 
may be converted into hydrogen chloride if the temperature within the BFB and CFB gasifier is above 800 
°C and 850 °C respectivly.  
For the cases where methyl chloride appears in the exit gas, it has to be removed before the gas can be used 
in the modern energy conversion technologies such as engines, turbines and fuel cells. The methyl chloride 
can be decomposed into hydrogen chloride by heating the exit gas to above 1000 °C or by making a partial 
oxidation of the gas after the gasification. As the methyl chloride is converted into hydrogen chloride, the gas 
has to be subsequently cleaned for hydrogen chloride. Due to this other cleaning methods for methyl chloride 
may be more attractive. Adsorbtion and catalytic steam reforming of methyl chloride could be attractive 
cleaning methods for methyl chloride however these technologies still remains unproven for this purpose. 
Methyl chloride is mainly formed at low temperatures (<300 °C) and due to this a thermal pretreatment of 
the biomass seems as an attractive cleaning method. The choice of the cleaning method will be dependent 
upon economics.

Project description   
Name: Morten Lund Rasmussen 
Studentnr: s103752 
ECTS-credits: 30 ECTS 
Supervisors: Peter Glarborg, Hao Wu, Hamid Hashemi 
Place: DTU Chemical Engineering 
Period: 04/04 2016 – 04/09 2016   
 
Background of the project 
Limitations in current hot gas cleaning methods for chlorine species from biomass gasification gas may be a 
major stumbling block for end use such as gas turbines, engines, and fuel cells that all require extremely low 
levels (1 ppm or less) of these contaminants. Efficient methods for removal of hydrogen chloride is based on 
scrubbing or adsorption on active material, but cleaning for organic forms of Cl released in biomass 
pyrolysis and gasification, i.e, CH3Cl, is currently more challenging. Unfortunately, thermal conversion of 
methyl chloride is currently not well understood and it is difficult to assess the fate of this species under 
gasification conditions.  
Objective  
The present project aims to develop a chemical kinetic model for thermal conversion of methyl chloride and 
to use the model to characterize the transformation of this species under gasification conditions. The 
practical implications of the results for gas clean-up requirements in biomass gasification will be outlined. 
Content 
A literature study covering biomass gasification and thermal conversion of methyl chloride will be 
conducted. Based on results from literature, as well as previous work at DTU Chemical Engineering, a 
chemical kinetic model for thermal conversion of methyl chloride will be established. The model will be 
validated against experimental data from literature on thermal decomposition and oxidation of methyl 
chloride (e.g., data from flow reactors, shock tubes, and laminar premixed flames). The developed model 
will be used for a parametric study of methyl chloride conversion under typical gasification conditions and 
the process implications for biomass gasification will be discussed. 
Timeline of project 
The timeline of the project is given in Table 1.

1 Introduction  
Today, most of the world energy supply is based on fossil fuels. The use of fossil fuels has raised serious 
environmental concerns due to the increasing level of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere and the greenhouse 
effect causing global warming. The environmental concerns have led to significant efforts in developing 
renewable energy technologies [1].  
Biomass is considered to be the renewable energy source with the highest potential for the energy needs of 
the future [2]. It is a major energy resource as it refers to all organic material which is produced by 
photosynthesis in the presence of sunlight. Biomass stores solar energy in the form of chemical bonds and by 
breaking the chemical bonds the stored energy can be released and utilized [3]. Thermal conversion 
processes such as combustion, gasification and pyrolysis are processes that can break the chemical bonds in 
biomass and release the stored energy. Biomass is a CO2 neutral energy source as the biomass roughly emits 
the same amount of carbon dioxide during conversion as it takes up during its growth due to photosynthesis 
[4]. The use of biomass as an energy source is currently providing 9.88% of the world’s energy supply [5] 
however the potential is far greater as it has been estimated that by 2050, 50 % of the world’s current energy 
supply of about 400 EJ per year can be meet by using biomass as a feedstock [4].  
However, biomass has a number of properties that makes it more difficult to handle and combust compared 
to fossil fuels. Biomass is mostly in a solid form and is therefore not compatible with modern energy 
conversion technologies like combustion engines and turbines as these are designed to handle clean gaseous 
and liquid fuels [6]. Due to this the biomass has to be converted into a liquid or gaseous fuel or used in 
combination with a steam cycle. Biomass has a low energy density which is the main problem in handling 
and transporting it and thus making biomass a local resource [6]. In connection with this, it is labor intensive 
to produce, harvest and transport energy crops as these are spread over large areas [7]. Biomass also has a 
significantly high content of inorganic components which makes it difficult to use as these might be released 
during conversion and deposit on heat transfer surfaces and enhance corrosion [6].  Furthermore biomass 
often needs pretreatment before it can be used. The degree of pretreatment depends on the type of biomass 
and the technology used for conversion [1]. The pretreatment involves the steps necessary to convert a 
harvested biomass into a usable fuel. One of the primary objectives in the pretreatment of biomass is to 
achieve a feedstock material that is homogeneous in size and composition [8]. Furthermore, pretreatment 
may also involve drying in order to get a fuel with a low moisture content [8]. 
The majority of biomass used in the energy production today is converted by combustion processes [9]. In 
conventional combustion power plants, the biomass is burned and the heat is subsequently used to generate 
steam. The steam is then used to drive a steam turbine which generates electricity. Biomass combustion is an 
established and mature technology which provides reliable operation [9]. However combustion of biomass 
has a rather low efficiency for electricity generation, as the generation of electricity from combustion of solid 
biomass is limited to the use of a steam turbine system [10]. The efficiency is typically 15 % for small plants 
and up to 30 % for newer and larger plants [2]. Heat is however often recovered to improve the overall 
thermal efficiency [10].  
An alternative to combustion of biomass is gasification which converts solid biomass into a fuel or synthesis 
gas. Since the gasification product is a fuel gas rather than a stream of hot combustion products, as produced 
from combustion, the opportunities for using biomass as an energy source is significantly increased. The gas 
produced from gasification can be burned to generate heat and electricity, or used as a feedstock to synthesis 
liquid fuels and chemicals such as diesel, gasoline methanol, ethanol, larger alcohols and pure hydrogen [11]. 
When the fuel gas is used to generate electric, it is consumed in gas engines, gas turbine or fuels cells which 
==PAGE:10==
 
Page 8 of 111 
 
are more efficient electricity generation technologies than the steam turbine system used for combustion 
systems. The efficiency of such systems is in the range of 30-40 % with the possibility of higher efficiency 
by using integrated combined cycles [10]. In an integrated gasification combined cycle the waste heat from 
the gasifier and the gas turbine is recovered and used to generate electricity in a steam turbine. Such a system 
can achieve an efficiency of about 60% [4]. The overall efficiency can even reach 60-90 % by making a 
combined heat and power production however this is also possible for the combustion of solid biomass [10].  
Despite the advantages of biomass gasification (energy efficiency and chemical synthesis), the technology is 
still in the developing phase as it faces some challenges [3]. The produced gas contains several 
contaminations, such as particulates, tar, alkali metal, sulfur, nitrogen and chlorine, which have to be 
removed before the gas can be used in gas engines, gas turbines, fuel cell or synthesis, as they may cause 
severe problems in the downstream application [9]. Among these contaminations, tar poses the greatest 
problem as the condensation of tar may block downstream pipelines and foul engines and turbines [12]. 
Therefore most of the literature on biomass gas cleaning has been concerned with tar removal [12]. Other 
contaminates such as chlorine have received much less attention due to its “small” concentration in the 
produced gas. Despite its low concentration compared to other contaminates, it can cause serious troubles in 
the downstream  process [13]. Chlorine levels as low as 20 ppm have been reported to significantly reduce 
the performance of fuel cells [13]. A chlorine level of less than 0.1 ppm is required to avoid performance loss 
in fuel cells [14]. Substantial corrosion of gas turbines blades may also occur at low chlorine concentration 
[13] and a tolerance of 0.5 ppm is reported for gas turbines [14]. Chlorine species are furthermore known to 
poison catalyst used for conversion of syngas into hydrogen [14][15] and other catalysts used in conversion 
of syngas [13]. For catalysts a chlorine content of about 1 ppm can be tolerated however a chlorine content 
of less than 1 ppm is desired [14]. Aside from this, chlorine species may result in deposition, enhanced 
corrosion and irreversible damage to the downstream processes [14].  
Chlorine is typically released during the pyrolysis as hydrogen chloride, alkali chloride and chlorinated 
hydrocarbons e.g. methyl chloride, CH3Cl [16]. Hydrogen chloride is the desired chlorine containing product 
as it is easily removed by using scrubbing process [16] or adsorption on active materials [17]. However, 
cleaning for chlorinated hydrocarbons such as methyl chloride is more challenging. The thermal conversion 
of methyl chloride is currently not well understood and this project aims to develop a kinetic model for the 
thermal conversion of methyl chloride. This model will be validated against experimental data from literature 
on thermal decomposition and oxidation of methyl chloride. The model will hereafter be used to characterize 
the conversion of methyl chloride under gasification conditions and the practical implications of the result 
for gas cleaning requirements will be discussed. In this project a description of the gasification process and 
the gasification technologies will be given to understand under which conditions the methyl chloride has to 
be thermally decomposed. Furthermore, experimental data on thermal decomposition and oxidation of 
methyl chloride from literature will be presented together with the most important reactions involved in the 
decomposition and oxidation of methyl chloride.  
 
 
==PAGE:11==
 
Page 9 of 111 
 
2 Biomass gasification  
The purpose of biomass gasification is to convert solid biomass into a combustible gas by using a gasifying 
agent such as air, oxygen or steam. Biomass gasification is normally carried out at high temperature (800-
1000 °C) and results in a gas consisting of hydrogen, carbon monoxide, methane, carbon dioxide, water 
vapor, nitrogen and various impurities [18]. After the impurities have been removed, the gas is typically used 
in combustion engines or gas turbines to generate electricity and heat. However the gas can also be used as a 
feedstock to produce liquid fuels or other chemicals [11]. Thus, the gas is more versatile to use than the 
original biomass as the gas can be used to produce electricity/heat, liquid fuels and other chemicals. 
The quality of the gas produced, is dependent on many factors which include the gasifier type, the biomass 
type, the operating condition and the gasifying agent used. Typically the quality of the gas is classified into 
two types, based on its energy content. The energy content is very dependent on the gasifying agent used. 
When air is used, a low quality gas with a heating value of 4-6 MJ/Nm3 is produced as the nitrogen in the air 
dilutes the gas. This type of gas is called producer gas. By using pure oxygen or steam as the gasifying agent, 
a gas with a higher quality is produced which has a heating value of 10-15 and 13-20 MJ/Nm3 respectively. 
This type of gas is called syngas. Generally, producer gas is suited for immediate use in gas engine or turbine 
to generate power while syngas is used as feedstock for liquid fuel or chemicals [17].  
Air is mostly used as the gasifying medium as the usage of oxygen and steam increases the cost and the 
complexity of the process [7] [12]. The disadvantage of using air is that nitrogen is introduced with it which 
dilutes the gas.  Consequently, a gas with a low heating value is obtained.  
2.1 Gasification zones and reactions  
Gasification is a complex process in which a fuel gas is obtained through a series of chemical reactions. A 
typical biomass gasification process can be divided into four steps: 
 
Drying to evaporate moisture  
 
Pyrolysis to thermally decompose the solid biomass into solid char, non-condensable and 
condensable gases 
 
Partial combustion to supply the necessary energy for drying, pyrolysis and the gasification of the 
solid char  
 
Reduction to gasify the solid char  
Each step will be explained in details in the following sections. 
2.1.1 Drying  
Drying consists in the evaporation of moisture from the biomass which typically has high moisture content. 
The energy required to evaporate the moisture from the biomass, is not recoverable in the gasification 
process and thus a certain amount of pre-drying of the biomass is needed before the biomass is fed to the 
gasifier [19]. The moisture content of the biomass should be between 10 and 15 % before it is fed to gasifier 
in order to produce a gaseous fuel with a reasonably high heating value [17]. The final drying of the biomass 
takes place inside the gasifier. The biomass is heated as it enters the gasifier and the water evaporates. The 
drying can be considered complete when the biomass reaches a temperature of 150 °C  [8]. The evaporated 
water may react with the biomass and the volatiles released in the pyrolysis. 
 
 
==PAGE:12==
 
Page 10 of 111 
 
2.1.2 Pyrolysis  
The pyrolysis involves thermal decomposition of the solid biomass structure by breaking the chemical bonds 
in the biomass at high temperature. The pyrolysis reaction takes place within a temperature range of 200–700 
°C [18] and progresses more rapidly as the temperature is increased [11]. The thermal decomposition of the 
solid biomass is accompanied by the release of volatiles in the form of non-condensable and condensable 
gases while a carbonaceous solid is formed [11]. When the biomass is heated the cellulose, hemicellulose 
and lignin, which are the main constituents of biomass, break down into volatile medium molecules and solid 
char [18]. During the secondary pyrolysis step some of the medium molecules may decompose into smaller 
compounds, non-condensable gases, while some of them aggregate and polymerize resulting in the formation 
of tar [18]. The overall pyrolysis reaction can be written as given in Eq. 2.1. 
 
𝐵𝑖𝑜𝑚𝑎𝑠𝑠→𝐻2 + 𝐶𝑂+ 𝐶𝑂2 + 𝐶𝐻4 + 𝐻2𝑂+ 𝐶ℎ𝑎𝑟+ 𝑇𝑎𝑟  
2.1 
Thus three different fractions are obtained from pyrolysis: a solid, liquid and gas fraction. The relative 
amount of each fraction is dependent on the kind of biomass and the process conditions [11].  
The solid fraction is called char and consists primarily of carbon (85 %) but it does also contain oxygen, 
hydrogen and some inert materials in the form of ashes [20]. The liquid fraction is usually called tar and 
consists of complex organic substances which condense at relative low temperatures. The gas fraction is the 
largest of the three fractions as it typically consists of 70-90 wt % of the fed material. The gas phase mainly 
consist of hydrogen, carbon monoxide, carbon dioxide and light hydrocarbons such as methane and  C2,C3 
hydrocarbons [8].  
2.1.2.1 Release of chlorine  
During the pyrolysis, chlorine is released in the form of methyl chloride, hydrogen chloride and alkali 
chloride [16]. Most studies have proposed that the majority of the chlorine released, during pyrolysis, is in 
the form of hydrogen chloride [21]. However a substantial amount of methyl chloride may be formed as 
Sailaukhanuly et al. [22] found that approximately 40 % of the chlorine released is in the form of methyl 
chloride. Furthermore, Saleh et al. [21], found that the majority of the chlorine released, during pyrolysis, 
was in the form of methyl chloride rather than hydrogen chloride in their study.  
Hamilton et al. [23] studied the release of methyl chloride from woody biomass and leaves. They proposed 
that pectin, which is a major component in the primary cell wall, contributed to the release of methyl 
chloride during pyrolysis. They proposed that pectin acts as a methyl donor to Cl- in the solid state to form 
methyl chloride. The reaction is a nucleophilic substitution reaction between the nucleophile chloride ion and 
the ester group in pectin [22]. The reaction can be seen in Figure 1. The gas release of methyl chloride from 
the reaction between pectin and chloride has been reported to be complete as the temperature reaches 300 °C 
[23]. Sailaukhanuly et al. [22] studied the formation of methyl chloride from the solid reaction between 
pectin and chloride ions found in alkali metal chlorides. They found that there was a linear correlation 
between the amount of chloride ions added to the pectin and the formation of methyl chloride when pectin 
was in great excess compared to chloride. This indicates that the reaction between the chloride ion and the 
methyl ester in the pectin is a pseudo first order SN2 reaction. Sailaukhanuly et al. [22] found that the 
formation of methyl chloride was independent of the cation in the alkali metal and the formation of methyl 
chloride was solely dependent of the amount of chloride. Furthermore, Sailaukhanuly et al. [22] reported that 
the formation of methyl chloride is very temperature dependent and a maximum of methyl chloride was 
formed at a temperature of 210 °C.   
 
==PAGE:13==
 
Page 11 of 111 
 
 
Figure 1: Formation of methyl chloride from pectin [22]. 
Egsgaard et al. [24] studied the formation of methyl chloride by heating lignin with KCl. A maximum of 
methyl chloride was formed between 600-800 °C by the reaction of lignin with KCl. Egsgaard et al. [24] 
proposed that the methoxy groups in the lignin act as a methyl donor in the formation of methyl chloride. 
Furthermore, Egsgaard et al. [24] suggested that this reaction is only a minor route for the formation of 
methyl chloride compared to the formation with pectin. Hamilton et al. [23] did also study the formation of 
methyl  by heating lignin in the presence of Cl-. They observed a substantial release of methyl chloride 
during their experiments at elevated temperatures but not at low temperatures. This seems to be in agreement 
with the maximum found by Egsgaard et al. [24].  In another study, Zsuzasanna et al. [25] investigated the 
release of methyl chloride from pyrolysis of polyvinylchloride-wood and wood component mixtures. Based 
on their research they proposed that the methoxy groups, which are presented in the lignin, act as methyl 
donor in the formation of methyl chloride under the consumption of hydrogen chloride. A proposal of the 
reaction is seen in Figure 2. 
 
Figure 2: Formation of methyl chloride from lignin [25] . 
Based on this there is small difference in the mechanism proposed by Egsgaard et al. [24] and Zsuzasanna et 
al. [25] however it is common for both mechanisms that the methoxy groups presented in the lignin act a 
methyl donor. 
The release of hydrogen chloride during pyrolysis of biomass was studied by Knudsen et al. They suggest 
that the release of HCl occur due to the reaction of KCl with the organic part of biomass. Zintl et al. further 
proposed that the release of hydrogen chloride occurred by the reaction between solid KCl and carboxylic 
groups in the biomass [21]. The reaction is seen in Eq. 2.2. 
 
𝑅−𝐶𝑂𝑂𝐻(𝑠) + 𝐾𝐶𝑙(𝑠) →𝑅−𝐶𝑂𝑂𝐾(𝑠) + 𝐻𝐶𝑙(𝑔) 
2.2 
2.1.3 Reduction  
The most important reactions that occur in the reduction zone are given in Eq. 2.3 to Eq. 2.6 [8]. The char 
produced in the pyrolysis zone mainly reacts with water and carbon dioxide to form carbon monoxide and 
hydrogen which are the main components in the final gaseous fuel [18]. The water shift reaction depicted in 
Eq. 2.6 also contributes to the formation of hydrogen through the reaction of water with carbon monoxide. 
The formed hydrogen may react with the solid char to form methane which is a small fraction of the finale 
==PAGE:14==
 
Page 12 of 111 
 
gas composition. As the reaction of solid char with water and carbon dioxide are the main reactions taking 
place in the reduction zone, the reduction step is globally endothermic. 
 
𝐶+ 𝐶𝑂2 ↔2𝐶𝑂;  Δ𝐻= 172 𝑘𝐽
𝑚𝑜𝑙  
2.3

𝑚𝑜𝑙  
2.4 
 
𝐶+ 2𝐻2 ↔𝐶𝐻4  ;Δ𝐻= −75 𝑘𝐽
𝑚𝑜𝑙   
2.5 
 
𝐶𝑂+ 𝐻2𝑂↔𝐶𝑂2 + 𝐻2  ; Δ𝐻= −41 𝑘𝐽
𝑚𝑜𝑙  
2.6 
The four reactions in Eq. 2.3 to Eq.2.6, are chemical equilibrium reactions and the temperature at which the 
reduction is carried out does therefore has a significant influence on the composition of the final gas [8]. 
2.1.4 Partial combustion  
The heat required for the endothermic heating, pyrolysis and reduction reactions to occur is supplied by the 
partial combustion of char and hydrogen, Eq. 2.7 to Eq. 2.9. The oxygen supplied to the process reacts with 
the char and the hydrogen to generate heat. The combustion is carried out under the lack of oxygen, with 
respect to the stoichiometric ratio, in order only to do a partial oxidation of the fuel. In practice both the char 
and the gaseous products formed during the pyrolysis are partially oxidized however for simplicity only the 
oxidation of char and hydrogen is shown here [8]. 
𝐶+ 𝑂2 →𝐶𝑂2 ;   Δ𝐻= −394 𝑘𝐽
𝑚𝑜𝑙 
2.7 
𝐶+ 1
2 𝑂2 →𝐶𝑂;  Δ𝐻= −111 𝑘𝑗
𝑚𝑜𝑙  
2.8

2.9 
The energy required for the pyrolysis and reduction may also be supplied from external heating instead of the 
internal heating caused by reactions in Eq. 2.7 to 2.9. When the energy is supplied from an external heating 
source the process is called allothermal gasification whereas internal heating of the gasifier is called auto-
thermal gasification [8]. 
3 Gasification technology  
The main reactors used in biomass gasification are fixed bed, fluidized bed reactor and entrained flow 
reactor. These four technologies will be described in details in this section. 
3.1 Fixed bed  
The fixed bed gasifier is the traditional reactor used for gasification [17]. The fixed bed gasifier can be 
designed in either a downdraft or an updraft configuration. The main difference between these two is the 
flow direction of the gasifying agent as it may be fed in co-current or counter-current with the solid biomass. 
Fixed bed gasifiers typically operate on a small scale to medium scale with capacities below 2 and 10 MW 
==PAGE:15==
 
Page 13 of 111 
 
for the downdraft and updraft respectively [6]. At larger scale fixed bed gasifiers experience problems with 
bridging of the biomass which lead to uneven gas flow [9].   
3.1.1 Updraft 
An updraft gasifier is one of the oldest and simplest of all the gasifier designs [26]. In the updraft gasifier the 
gasifying agent is fed from the bottom and passes upward through the combustion zone while the biomass is 
fed from top, as shown in Figure 3. Thus, the biomass and the gasifying agent flow in countercurrent. As the 
biomass enters the gasifier it is dried by the up flowing hot gas. After drying the biomass descends through 
the gasifier and is subsequently pyrolyzied to give solid char, which continues to move down to the reduction 
section, while the volatile species are carried upwards by the up flowing gas stream. The solid char, formed 
from the pyrolysis, is hereafter gasified and the remaining solid char is combusted at the bottom by the 
incoming air to give the required energy for the reduction reactions, pyrolysis and the drying step. As the gas 
moves upwards from the combustion zone, the temperature decreases due to the endothermic process taking 
place and thus the gas leaves at a relative low temperature, between 200 and 300 °C. [17] The low 
temperature of the exiting gas ensures that the overall energy efficiency of the updraft gasifier is high [17]. A 
disadvantage of this design is that a considerable amount of tar formed in the pyrolysis zone, easily leaves 
the gasifier with the gas stream [9]. This is because the gas does not pass through the combustion zone where 
the tar could be thermally decomposed. Thus, the gas produced from the updraft gasifier contains large 
quantities of tar [18]. The gas produced from the updraft gasifier is therefore suited to be burnt in a furnace 
or boiler where a clean gas is not required [26]. 
 
Figure 3: Schematic of updraft gasifier [17]. 
3.1.2 Downdraft  
In the downdraft gasifier the biomass and the gasifying agent flow in the same direction which can be seen in 
Figure 4. The biomass is fed from the top while the gasifying agent is introduced in the middle of the gasifier 
where it meets the pyrolysis products and forms the combustion section. Afterwards, the gaseous species and 
the solid char move down into the reduction zone where the gaseous species are reduced by the char. Thus, 
this design results in a reversed order of the reduction and combustion zones compared to the updraft 
configuration. The advantage of this design is that the volatiles, released during the pyrolysis, pass through 
the combustion zone where the tar is rapidly decomposed due to the high temperatures, 800-1200 °C [11].  
Tar conversion of above 99% can be achieved in this way [11]. Because of this a gas with a low content of 
tar, compared to that for the updraft, is produced. Due to this the downdraft gasifier works well with a 
==PAGE:16==
 
Page 14 of 111 
 
combustion engine which needs cleaner gas compared to the furnace/boilers [26]. The disadvantage of the 
downdraft gasifier is that the overall energy efficiency is low because the gas leaves the gasifier at a relative 
high temperature of 700-1000 °C as the gas stream is not cooled on its way out [7][11][17]. Furthermore, the 
downdraft gasifier also needs fuels with a moisture content of less than 20 % in order to achieve 
temperatures high enough in the combustion zone to crack the tar [11] while the updraft gasifier can use 
fuels with a moisture content up to 60 % [18]. 
 
Figure 4: Schematic of downdraft gasifier [17]. 
3.2 Fluidized bed  
Fluidized bed gasifiers are widely used for biomass gasification [18] and are currently the most promising 
technology in biomass gasification [8]. Essentially, all of the large scale facilities built and tested in the 
1990’s are fluidized bed gasifiers [9]. A fluidized bed is made of a bed of inert granular material which is 
fluidized by the gasifying agent. Under this condition the bed of granular material behaves like a liquid 
which is continuously agitated by the gasifying agent flowing through it. The turbulence within the bed 
results in high heat and mass transfer rates and thereby good mixing is achieved [8]. Thus, almost uniform 
conditions across the bed are obtained [8]. One problem with the fluidized bed is that inorganic components 
in the biomass may react with the inert bed material and form agglomerated particles which stops the 
fluidization [18]. Furthermore, the gas exiting the gasifier typically contains high level of particulates due to 
the turbulence in the bed [9]. 
There are two types of fluidized bed: bubbling fluidized bed, BFB, and circulating fluidized bed, CFB, and 
these will be further described below. 
3.2.1 Bubbling fluidized bed 
A schematic drawing of a BFB and CFB are seen in Figure 5. In the BFB the biomass is fed to the fluidized 
bed where it comes into contact with the hot bed material. The hot bed material quickly heats the biomass to 
the bed temperature where after it rapidly undergoes drying, pyrolysis, partial combustion and reduction 
[18][19]. Thus, most of the conversion of the biomass takes place within the bed however a part of the 
gasification reactions are completed in the freeboard due to entrained particles and thermal decomposing of 
tar [7] [8]. 
==PAGE:17==
 
Page 15 of 111 
 
 
Figure 5: Schematic of bubbling fluidized bed (left) and circulating fluidized bed (right) [7]. 
BFB is usually operated at temperature between 790 and 870 °C [11] and the gas typically leaves the gasifier 
at relative high temperatures of approximating 800 °C [7]. The BFB gasifier is appropriate for medium scale 
unit of 25 MW or less [10] . 
3.2.2 Circulating fluidized bed 
The CFB is an extension of the BFB. The CFB operates in a more turbulent fluidization mode with higher 
gas velocities compared to the BFB. In the CFB the gas velocity is high enough to entrain large amounts of 
solid particles, including bed material and char, with the product gas [18]. The entrained solid material is 
separated from the gas by a cyclone and recycled to the fluid bed. In the CFB there is thus no well-defined 
interface between the bed and the freeboard as there is in the BFB. The CFB was developed to improve the 
carbon conversion efficiency compared with BFB as some of the solid char was entrained [7]. The CFB is 
usually operated at temperatures between 800 and 1000 °C and the temperature of the leaving gas is similar 
to that of the operating temperature [18]. 
The CFB applies to small scale units of a few MW and up to very large units of 100 MW [10]. For large 
scale applications the CFB is the preferable choice as it is easy to scale, has high throughput and can use 
various types of biomass as a fuel [18].  
3.3 Entrained bed 
In an entrained bed, the biomass in the form of fine particles (0.1-1 mm) is fed from the top of the reactor 
together with the gasifying agent which can be seen from Figure 6 [8]. The particles are entrained in the 
gasifying medium and flow through the reactor in an almost plug flow mode [19]. The gasifying medium is 
rapidly heated to the reactor temperature upon entering while the solids it heated up more slowly due to the 
larger heat capacity [19]. The entrained flow gasifier operates at very high temperatures of 1300-1500 °C 
[8][11] and the gasifying agent rapidly reacts with the volatiles and char upon entering the reactor which 
results in short residence times (few seconds) [19][18]. Due to the high temperature within the reactor the 
produced gas has a very low content of tar [11]. The entrained bed gasifier is appropriate for large scale units 
as it has capacity of several hundred MW [18]. 
The entrained bed gasifier has been widely used in coal gasification; however, they are not recommended to 
be used for biomass gasification. This is because it is difficult to obtain uniform fine particle of biomass 
which is required for the entrained flow gasifier. Furthermore, the ash in the biomass may melt and aggregate 
at the high operating temperature and form corrosive slag [18].  
==PAGE:18==
 
Page 16 of 111 
 
 
Figure 6: Schematic of entrained flow gasifier [19]. 
Characteristics for the different types of gasifiers presented are given in the Table 2 .

3.4 Status  
In 2000, a survey of gasifier manufacturers was conducted and from this it was found that 75% of gasifiers 
that was offered commercially were downdraft, 20% were fluid beds which includes circulating fluid beds, 
2.5% were updraft and 2.5% were other types [2]. Thus, the downdraft and the fluid beds are probably the 
most used gasifiers. 
The gasifiers may operate under atmospheric or pressurized conditions. Pressurized gasifiers have several 
advantages compared to atmospheric gasifiers. Under pressurized conditions smaller process equipment is 
needed for a given throughput as the gas volumes are smaller.  The gas produced from a pressurized gasifiers 
can be fed directly into a gas turbine which eliminates the need for compression of the produced gas [11]. 
Furthermore, the overall efficiency is higher for a pressurized gasifier than for an atmospheric gasifier [7]. 
The pressurized gasification does however also have several disadvantages. Feeding of solid into a 
pressurized system is complex and costly [7]. The capital cost of a pressurized gasifier is much larger than an 
atmospheric system [7]. Furthermore operating gasifiers at elevated pressured increases the complexity of 
the plant [18]. The advantages of pressurized gasification systems do not overcome the disadvantages and 
atmospheric gasifiers are therefore more commonly used [11]. This assessment is further supported by 
Bridgwater [2] as he states that “No company is known to be developing pressurized systems for downdraft, 
updraft or entrained bed gasifiers for biomass feedstocks and it is difficult to imagine that such a technology 
could ever be developed into a commercial product due to the inherent problems of scale, tar removal and 
==PAGE:19==
 
Page 17 of 111 
 
cost”. Furthermore Bridgwater [2] states that pressurized fluidized bed gasifiers are less attractive to the 
market than atmospheric fluidized beds due to the more complex operation and the additional cost related to 
the use of pressurized equipment. 
4  Gas cleaning for chlorine 
The technology for gas cleaning can be divided into two categories: cold and hot gas cleaning. Cold gas 
cleaning is the conventional technology; however, hot gas cleaning is more attractive as it is more energy 
efficient [18]. In cold gas cleaning the gas stream is cooled and cleaned after which it is reheated to the 
desired temperature of the downstream application. In hot gas cleaning the cooling and reheating of the gas 
stream is avoided and therefore this technology is more energy efficient. The hot gas cleaning does however 
face significant technical challenges while the cold gas cleaning is a mature technique which is highly 
efficient [13]. There is however a trend to move towards hot gas cleaning [14]. 
Chlorine in biomass mainly occurs as alkali metal salts which vaporize at high temperature condition within 
the gasifier and react with water vapor to form hydrogen chloride [13]. Hydrogen chloride is commonly 
reported to be the major form of chlorine in the produced gas [3][13] [17]; however, the gas will also contain 
methyl chloride as it is formed during the pyrolysis [21]. Hydrogen chloride is the desired chlorine contained 
species as it easily can be removed from the fuel gas by scrubbing process or adsorption on active material 
[16]. The typical concentration of hydrogen chloride in the produced gas may range from 20 to 200 ppm 
however the concertation may be significantly higher if biomass with a high chlorine content is used [12]. In 
this section the removal of hydrogen chlorine by wet scrubbing (cold gas cleaning), absorption on active 
material (hot gas cleaning) and removal of methyl chloride by steam reforming will be described.  
4.1 Wet scrubber  
Most commercial operations use cold gas cleaning for the removal of hydrogen chloride [13]. The cold gas 
cleaning is mainly based on wet scrubbers [13]. In a wet scrubber the hot gas from the gasifier passed up 
through the scrubber while water is dispersed from the top and flows downwards in a countercurrent flow. 
The gas is thus brought into contact with the water and the contaminants in the gas, which are highly soluble 
in water (NH3, HCl, H2S and SO2), are rapidly absorbed and dissolved in the water [3]. It should be noted 
that methyl chloride passes unrestricted through the wet scrubber as it is not absorbed in the water [27]. 
Beside the soluble species, other contaminates such as tar, particulate matter and alkali metals will also be 
captured in the wet scrubber. As the gas rises in the wet scrubber the gas is cooled and tar vapors and alkali 
compounds condense and are subsequently absorbed in the water [3]. One of the advantages of wet 
scrubbing is that it is capable of removing several contaminants in contrast to other downstream units that 
only remove one contaminant.   
The water leaving the wet scrubber has a high content of tar and does therefore enter a settling tank where 
water-insoluble tars are separated from the water. The water can hereafter be recirculated to the scrubber. 
Eventually both soluble tars and other soluble substances are accumulated in the water and this reduces the 
effectiveness of the wet scrubber and therefore the water stream has to be cleaned or clean water has to be 
feed to the scrubber. The wastewater, produced from the scrubber, cannot be discharged to the environment 
before it has been cleaned [13]. The wastewater produced is one of the big disadvantages of using wet 
scrubbers as a new stream with contaminates is created [18]. However efficient wastewater treatment has 
been developed, which has a cleaning efficiency of 99.98 %, and the water can thereby be discharged 
without restriction after the treatment [6]. 
==PAGE:20==
 
Page 18 of 111 
 
There exist many different type of wet scrubber such as spray, packed bed scrubbers, impingement 
scrubbers, and ventures scrubbers which brings the gas and the water into contact by using different 
techniques [3].   
4.2 Adsorption 
Hydrogen chloride is typically removed in hot gas cleaning by using solid sorbents [13]. The hot gas passes 
through a packed bed reactor which is filled with absorber material. The hydrogen chloride is thereby 
removed from the gas as it is adsorbed on the surface of the solid sorbent. The solid sorbent has to be 
removed or regenerated when the surface is saturated with hydrogen chloride. The adsorption of hydrogen 
chloride have been reported to be most efficient between 500 °C and 550 °C [13]. Serval different materials 
have been suggested to be used as a solid sorbent for the removal of hydrogen chloride. Factors to be 
considered when choosing a solid sorbent is the affinity towards hydrogen chloride, the stability of the solid 
sorbent at evaluated temperatures so it does not decompose, the tolerance of the sorbent towards other 
contaminations and large surface area of the sorbent [13] [14]. Activated carbon, alumina and common alkali 
oxides have commonly been used in packed bed reactors for the removal of hydrogen chloride [13]. 
Currently there is a focus on developing solid sorbents based on sodium carbonate and calcium carbonate 
[14].  
There are very limited data available for the removal of methyl chloride by sodium carbonate based sorbents 
[28] and no reports on the efficiency of calcium carbonate based sorbents have been found. Zamansky et al. 
[28] studied the removal of halogenated species from flue gas. They found that the removal of methyl 
chloride was promoted by sending the flue gas up through a packed bed with sodium carbonate. Further, 
investigation is needed to identify if this will be an effect method for capturing methyl chloride under 
gasification conditions.  
An alternative to packed bed sorption is to directly inject sorbent into the hot gas stream. 80 % removal of 
hydrogen chloride has been obtained in this way by using calcium based powders [13]. A second alternative 
is to blend the sorbent with the fuel in a fluidized bed gasifier thus in situ removal of hydrogen chloride is 
achieved [14].  
4.3 Steam reforming  
Catalytic steam reforming of methyl chloride is an alternative method to remove methyl chloride from the 
gas. The reaction for steam reforming is seen in Eq. 4.1. 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻2𝑂→2𝐻2 + 𝐶𝑂+ 𝐻𝐶𝑙 
4.1 
Richardson et al. [29] studied catalyst candidates for the steam reforming of methyl chloride. They found 
that the most actively catalysts for the stream reforming of methyl chloride are Rh, Pd, Pt, Cu, Re, Ir, Ri, Ni 
and Co listing with decreasing activity. Extremely high conversion (>0.998) of methyl chloride was obtained 
with Pt, Rh and Pd at a temperature of 600 °C.  
Ortego et al. [30] demonstrated that the deactivation of the catalyst mainly occurred due to the formation of 
coke on the catalyst surface. Furthermore, they showed that the formation of coke could be minimized by 
operating at temperatures above 750 °C formation and that the catalyst could be regenerated by using steam.  
The catalytic steam reforming of methyl chloride seems to be an attractive process to remove methyl 
chloride as high conversion are possible and useful products are formed. However the use of this technology 
to remove methyl chloride from gas produced from gasifiers still remains unproved [24].  
==PAGE:21==
 
Page 19 of 111 
 
5 Experimental data for methyl chloride  
To validate the kinetic model of methyl chloride, which is going to be developed during this project, 
experimental data are required. This section will describe the experimental data on thermal decomposition 
and oxidation of methyl chloride which have been reported in the literature. Furthermore, the usefulness of 
these data to validate the kinetic model will be assessed. The experimental data are divided into four 
categories: plug flow reactor, shock tube, laminar burning velocity and structure of premixed laminar flames.  
5.1 Plug flow reactor 
In a plug flow reactor the reagents are pumped into a tube, where they react along the length of the reactor. 
For isothermal conditions the mixture travels with a constant velocity through the reactor which means that 
the length of the reactor easily can be converted into residence/reaction time. By varying the flow rate or the 
length of the reactor, data for different residence times can be achieved. At the end of reactor the exit gas is 
analyzed to determine the concentration of the involved species. Note that the reactants are rapidly mixed 
before they enter the flow reactor to ensure that the reaction is kinetically controlled. By far the most 
experimental work in connection with thermal decomposition and oxidation of methyl chloride has been 
carried out in flow reactors. The various experimental data obtained by different research groups will be 
presented in this section. Furthermore, the major products from the thermal decomposition and oxidation of 
methyl chloride will be identified based on the experimental data.  
5.1.1 Thermal conversion  
Won et al. [31] studied the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in a hydrogen atmosphere. The 
experiments were conducted at a total pressure of 1 atm while the residence time and the temperature were 
varied from 0.3-2 s and 998-1223 K respectively. The experimental results obtained by Won et al. [31] are 
depicted in Figure 7. 
 
Figure 7: Thermal conversion of methyl chloride at 850 °C plotted as a function of residence time (left). Thermal conversion 
of methyl chloride as a function of temperature at a fixed residence time of 1.0 s (right). Symbols are experimental data while 
the lines are predictions of Won et al. [31]. Initial concentrations were 4 % CH3Cl and 96 % H2.  
The experimental results show that hydrogen chloride and methane are the major products from the thermal 
decomposition while ethane and ethylene are minor products. From the experimental data it can also be 
==PAGE:22==
 
Page 20 of 111 
 
observed that the chlorine balance is off and this indicates that chlorinated species, which are not measured, 
are formed during the decomposition of methyl chloride or the measurement of hydrogen chloride is 
uncertain. The data set from Won et al. [31] seems useful to use in the validation as the temperatures 
investigated is similar to the temperatures within gasifiers. Under these experimental conditions there are an 
excess of hydrogen compared with methyl chloride and that will also be the case under gasification 
conditions.    
Hung et al. [32] studied the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in a nitrogen atmosphere. The 
experiments were conducted in an isothermal flow reactor at a constant pressure of 1.14 atm and with 
temperatures varying from 1000 to 1350 K. In the study of Hung et al. [32], the residence time within the 
reactor varies with the temperature. The residence time is known to be 19.5 and 16 ms at 1100 and 1350 K 
respectively. This information can be used to calculate the residence time for the entire temperature range. 
The experimental results obtained by Hung et al. [32] are depicted in Figure 8.  
 
Figure 8: Thermal conversion of methyl chloride as a function of temperature. The initial concentration of methyl chloride is 
5100 ppm [32].  
From the experimental data it can be seen that the major products for the decomposition of methyl chloride 
are methane, ethylene and acetylene while a small amount of vinyl chloride is formed. Unfortunately, it is 
not clear from the experimental data what the majority of chlorine is converted into which is important for 
this study. However, the data set is still useful for the validation as it covers a representative temperature 
range for gasification.  
Weissman et al. [33] studied the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride at temperatures of 1260 and 1310 
K. In their study additives in the form of CH4, C2H2, C2H4 and C6H6 were initially added to the methyl 
chloride mixture in order to investigate the influence of these species on the thermal decomposition of 
methyl chloride. The initially conditions of the experiments performed by Weissman et al. are given in Table 
3.  However the total pressures of the experiments are not stated in their paper but it might be the sum of the 
partial pressures given in Table 3.  As the total pressure is not known the experimental data of Weissman et 
al. [33] will not be used in the validation. Weissman et al. [33] reported that carbon, methane, hydrogen, 
ethylene, acetylene and hydrogen chloride is among the major products from the thermal decomposition of 
methyl chloride.  
There seems to be consistency between the experimental data obtained by the three groups presented in this 
section even though they studied the decomposition of methyl chloride under different conditions. From the 
experimental data presented the major products from the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride seems to 
be hydrogen chloride, methane, ethylene and acetylene.

5.1.2 Oxidation of methyl chloride 
Bozzelli et al. [34] studied the oxidation of methyl chloride in hydrogen/oxygen/argon mixtures. The 
experiments were carried out in an isothermal tubular flow reactor at 1 atmosphere and with temperatures 
between 1098 and 1273 K. Some of experimental data obtained by Bozzelli et al. [34]  are given in Figure 9 
and Figure 10. 
 
Figure 9: Normalized concentration of the involved species in the decomposition of methyl chloride as a function of residence 
time at a fixed temperature of 1173 K. Reactant ratio Ar:CH3Cl:H2:O2=96:2:1:1 [34]. 
From the experimental data of Bozzelli et al. [34], it is seen that the chlorine from methyl chloride is almost 
fully converted into hydrogen chloride and only a small amount of other chlorinate carbonate species is 
formed. Furthermore, it is seen that methane, acetylene and carbon monoxide are among the major products. 
The experimental data of Bozzelli et al. [34] covers a temperature range which is representative for 
gasification. Furthermore, these data are obtained under reducing conditions which there also will be under 
gasification. Due to this these data is useful in validating the model.  
 
 
==PAGE:24==
 
Page 22 of 111 
 
 
Figure 10: Normalized concentration of the involved species in the decomposition of methyl chloride as a function of 
temperature at a fixed residence time of 1 s. Reactant ratio Ar:CH3Cl:H2:O2=96:2:1:1 [34]. 
Hung et al. [32] studied the oxidation of methyl chloride under very fuel-lean conditions in an isothermal 
flow reactor. In the experiments the concentration of methyl chloride was initially 4800±100 ppm while the 
rest of the mixture consisted of air. The experiments were conducted at a constant pressure of 1.14 atm and 
with temperatures varying from 1000 to 1350 K. In the experiment, the residence time is a function of the 
temperature. The residence time is known to be 19.5 and 16 ms at 1100 and 1350 K respectively. From this 
the residence time for the entire temperature range can be calculated. The experimental data of Hung et al. 
[32] is depicted in Figure 11. 
Figure 11: Oxidation of methyl chloride under fuel lean conditions. Experimental data from Hung et al. [32].  
From the experimental data of Hung et al. [32], it can be seen that carbon monoxide and carbon dioxide is 
among the major products under fuel-lean conditions, while the hydrocarbons are minor products. This is 
because the hydrocarbons are rapidly oxidized into carbon monoxide and carbon dioxide under fuel lean 
conditions. These experimental data may not be so useful for the validation of the mechanism as these 
conditions is very different from that of gasification. Under gasification there will be reducing conditions and 
not oxidizing conditions. 
Roesler et al. [35] studied the inhibition effect of methyl chloride on oxidation of CO at atmospheric 
pressure. In this study, trace amount of methyl chloride was added to a moist CO mixture. The experiments 
performed by Roesler et al. [35] are quite different from the other experiment data presented as only trace 
amount of methyl chloride is present in these experiments. Furthermore, the gas consists of carbon 
monoxide, water and nitrogen and thereby the gas composition is somewhat similar to the gas composition 
==PAGE:25==
 
Page 23 of 111 
 
within a gasifier. Due to this the data is very useful in the validation of the mechanism. The initial conditions 
for the experiments conducted by Roesler et al. [35] are given in Table 4. The experiments were conducted 
in an adiabatic reactor. 
Table 4: Initial conditions for experiment performed by [35] 
Mixture 
T [K]

The experimental data obtained for mixture 2 are depicted in Figure 12. 
 
Figure 12: Oxidation of CO in the presence of methyl chloride. Experimental data taken from Roesler et al. [35].  
From the experimental data of Roesler et al. [35], it can be seen that the methyl chloride completely inhibits 
the oxidation of carbon monoxide. First, when nearly all the methyl chloride is consumed, the oxidation of 
carbon monoxide occurs. Under these conditions it can be seen that the major products for the oxidation of 
methyl chloride are hydrogen chloride, ethylene, formaldehyde and carbon dioxide.  
Granada et al. [36][37] both studied the oxidation of methyl chloride, in the presence of oxygen, and the 
thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in the absence of oxygen, in a flow reactor. The experiments were 
conducted at constant temperature and pressure of 980 °C and 0.678 atm respectively. Under the oxidative 
conditions, the initial gas consisted of 7.32 % methyl chloride, 2.05 % oxygen and 90.6 % argon while the 
initial gas in the absence of oxygen consisted of 7.47 % methyl chloride and 92.5 % argon. Granada et al.  
[36][37] present their experimental data as a function of the reactor length. In their article they states that the 
residence time range investigated is 50-250 ms however this does not agree with the residence time calculate 
based on length of the reactor and the mean gas flow velocities given in the article. Due to this there is an 
inconstancy in the data and the residence of the experimental data is not certain. Therefore these data will not 
be used in the validation. Granada et al. [36][37] report that hydrogen chloride, ethylene, acetylene are 
among the major products for both the thermal conversion and the oxidation of methyl chloride. 
From the experimental data there seems to be a consensus about the major products from the oxidation of 
methyl chloride. Generally, the major products under fuel rich condition are hydrogen chloride, methane, 
==PAGE:26==
 
Page 24 of 111 
 
ethylene, acetylene and carbon monoxide. However under fuel lean condition carbon dioxide becomes one of 
the major products.  
5.2 Shock tube 
The shock tube reactor has typically been used to study high temperature kinetics of complex and elementary 
reactions. A conventional shock tube consists of a long tube in which a diaphragm separates a high pressure 
driver gas from a low pressure test gas. When the diaphragm ruptures a shock wave propagates through the 
test gas. The shock wave increases the temperature and the pressure of the test gas which initiates reaction. 
The reaction can be studied either as the shock wave passes through the gas the first time (incident shock) or 
after the shock wave have been reflected from the end of the wall (reflected shock). The temperature and 
pressure at which the reaction is studied can be controlled by changing the pressure of the driver gas, the test 
gas and the gas used as driver gas [38]. In this section experimental data for ignition delay of methyl chloride 
mixtures obtained in shock tube will be presented. Furthermore, data on the thermal decomposition of methyl 
chloride obtained in shock tube will also be presented.  
Miller et al. [39] studied the ignition delay for mixtures containing methyl chloride. The experiments were 
performed in a shock tube reactor at temperature from 1300 K to 1600 K and with a pressure behind the 
reflected shock of 2 atm. The experimental results obtained by Miller et al. [39] are depicted in Figure 13.  
 
Figure 13: Experimental data on shock tube ignition delay time with a pressure behind the reflected shock of 2 atm [39]. 
The data obtained by Miller et al. [39] is useful in the validation as it is some of the only data that covers a 
temperature range representative for the downdraft and entrained bed gasifiers.   
Shin et al. [40] studied the effect of methyl chloride on the ignition of methane mixtures. Shin et al. [40]  
measured the ignition delay for stoichiometric mixtures of CH4/O2/Ar in the presence and absence of methyl 
chloride within a temperature and pressure range of 1588-1920 K and 1.05-1.39 bar respectively. The 
composition of the mixtures, investigated by Shin et al. [40], are given in Table 5.  
Table 5: Composition of the mixtures investigated by [40]

Shin et al. [40] defined the ignition delay time as the time interval between the arrival of the reflected shock 
and the time where the OH concentration reaches its maximum. The experimental data of Shin et al. [40] is 
depicted in Figure 14. 
 
Figure 14: Ignition delay times for the mixtures in Table 5 [40]. 
From the experimental data it is seen that for low methyl chloride loading (mixture 2) the effect on the 
ignition delay times are very small. The effect does however become more significant as the loading of 
methyl chloride is increased. As only small concentrations of methyl chloride are present in the mixtures, the 
reactions controlling the ignition may be the ones concerning the C-H-O species and not the reactions 
involving chlorinated species. As a consequence of this these data may not be so useful in validating a 
mechanism for methyl chloride.  
Frenklach et al. [41] studied the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in a shock tube reactor. In this 
type of experiment the test gas is heated by the shock wave for a short period of time where after the gas is 
rapidly cooled. The gas is subsequently analyzed. The gas mixture investigated contained initially 3% methyl 
chloride, 10 % nitrogen and 87 % argon. The experiments were performed within a temperature and pressure 
range of 1345-2472 K of 2.40-3.60 bar respectively and with a constant reaction time of 500±50 μs. These 
data are however not very useful for the validation part of the chemical mechanism as the gasifiers does not 
operate at such high temperatures as the one investigated by Frenklach et al. [41]. 
Similar Kondo et al. [42] studied the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in a shock tube reactor. 
However they did not measure the concentration of stable species in the gas but measured the concentration 
of methyl radicals as a function of the reaction time. It is not very useful to validate a detail reaction 
mechanism against these experimental data as only the concentration of methyl radicals have been measured. 
5.3 Laminar burning velocity 
In this section the laminar burning velocity for a variety of methyl chloride and methane mixtures 
determined by different research groups will be presented. The burning velocity is defined as the velocity, 
normal to the flame front, at which the unburnt gas enters the front. The burning velocity can be determined 
by a variety of methods. It can for example be determined by the Burners methods in which a flame is 
stabilized on a vertical burner [43]. The premixed gas enters the vertical burner from below and flows 
laminar through the burner while the flame is being held stationary by the flow of premixed gas [43]. The 
laminar burning velocity is an important physio-chemical property of a combustible mixture as the square of 
the burning velocity is proportional to the overall rate of combustion. Thus large burning velocities normally 
==PAGE:28==
 
Page 26 of 111 
 
indicate that the combustion reactions are fast while small burning velocities indicate slow combustion rates. 
The burning velocity depends on the mixture of the fuel, the temperature and the pressure [44].  
For a premixed mixture of methyl chloride, methane, oxygen and inert species the overall stoichiometry can 
be expressed as given in Eq. 5.1. 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 1
𝑅𝐶𝐻4 + [2
𝑅+ 3
2] 𝑂2 →[1 + 1
𝑅] 𝐶𝑂2 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙+ [1 + 2
𝑅] 𝐻2𝑂  
5.1 
Where R is the molar ratio of methyl chloride to methane in the mixture. The equivalence ratio is defined as 
the actual fuel/oxygen ratio to the stoichiometric fuel/oxygen ratio as given in Eq. 5.2 [45]. 
 
𝜙=
(2
𝑅+ 3
2)
𝑎𝑐𝑡𝑢𝑎𝑙 𝑂2𝑖𝑛 𝑚𝑖𝑥𝑢𝑟𝑒 
5.2 
 
For 𝜙= 1 the actual oxygen used correspond to the stoichiometric requirement while 𝜙< 1 and 𝜙> 1 
corresponds to fuel lean and fuel rich mixtures respectively. The burning velocity is often presented as a 
function of equivalence ratio and with the molar ratio of methyl chloride to methane as a parameter [44]. Due 
to this, it was important to define these two terms before presenting the experimental data. 
Valeiras et al. [44] studied the laminar burning velocities for several chlorinated species including that of 
methyl chloride mixtures with methane and air. In their study they used the Bunsen cone method to 
determine the burning velocities for premixed flames at atmospheric pressure. The uncertainty for the 
determination of the burning velocity was reported to be between 10 and 20 % by using this technique. 
Valeiras et al. [44] determined the burning velocities for mixtures with equivalence ratio of 0.8 to 1.8 and 
molar ratio of methyl chloride to methane of 0.48 to 2.0 at pre-combustion temperatures from 20 to 140 °C.  
Chelliah et al. [45] studied the burning velocity of CH3Cl/CH4/air and CH3Cl/air mixtures by using a 
counterflow configuration. In this study the burning velocities were determined for stoichiometric mixtures 
of CH3Cl/CH4/air with increasing concentration of methyl chloride. Furthermore, burning velocities were 
determined for CH3Cl/CH4/air mixtures with R=1 and equivalence ratios between 0.75 and 1.5. The 
experiments, performed by Chelliah et al. [45], were carried out at atmospheric pressure and at a temperature 
of 298 K. 
Shin et al. [46] studied the burning velocities of CH4/CH3Cl/O2/N2 premixed flames under oxygen enriched 
condition. Instead of using air as an oxidizer, a gas with an oxygen mole fraction between 0.21 and 0.35 was 
used. The rest of the oxidizing gas consisted of nitrogen. The burning velocities were determined at room 
temperature and atmospheric pressure by using the Bunsen nozzle flame technique. Burning velocities for 
mixtures with equivalence ratio of 0.7 to 1.3 and molar ratio of methyl chloride to methane of 0 to 1 was 
determined during this study. 
Burning velocity for these three groups are depicted in Figure 15 for R=1 and temperatures of 293-300 K. 
From the experimental data it can be seen that there is a relative large uncertainty in determining the burning 
velocity. However the experimental data from Chelliah et al. [45] and Valeiras et al. [44] shows similar trend 
and the “real” burning velocity may lie in between these two data sets. The experimental values determined 
by Shin et al. [46]  do not catch the same trend as the two other groups. The technique used by Shin et al. 
[46] to measure the burning velocity  may therefore not produce reliable results.  
==PAGE:29==
 
Page 27 of 111 
 
Even though the uncertainty in determining the burning velocity is relatively large, the experimental data 
will still be used in the validation in order to see if the kinetic model is able to reproduce the same trends.  
 
Figure 15: Burning velocity of CH3Cl/CH4/air flame with R=1 as a function of equivalence ratio. The circles are experimental 
data from Valeiras et al. [44] while the squares and the diamonds are from Chelliah et al. [45] and Shin et  al. [46] 
respectively.  
5.4 Chemical structure of premixed flat flame 
When studying the chemical structure of a premixed flat flames, a flame is stabilized on top of a porous flat 
flame burner through which the premixed reaction mixture is fed [38]. During such an experiment the 
temperature and the concentration of the chemical species are measured as a function of the distance from 
the surface of the burner. Three different groups have studied the chemical structure of premixed flat flames 
of methyl chloride mixtures.  
Karra et al. [47] studied the structure of premixed, atmospheric pressure, flat flames of CH3Cl/CH4/O2/Ar 
mixtures. The premixed mixture studied contained 13.4 % methyl chloride, 26.6 % methane, 37.6 % oxygen 
and 23.3 % argon.  
Miller et al. [48] studied the flame structure of premixed CH3Cl/CH4/air mixtures at atmospheric pressure. 
They conducted experiments for five different mixtures and the conditions of these can be seen in Table 6. 
Table 6: Composition of the premixed mixture for the study made by [48]

fuel lean conditions. In their study, mixtures with a molar ratio of methyl chloride to methane of 0, 0.25 and 
0.5 was investigated. All experiments were conducted at an equivalence ratio of 0.79.  
The data from these groups were all obtained at atmospheric pressure which results in a very short flame 
(few millimeters). Consequently, the uncertainties of these experiments are very large and thus these 
experimental data will not be used in the validation of the mechanism developed.
