6 Kinetics of methyl chloride  
In this section the most important reactions for the thermal decomposition and oxidation of methyl chloride 
will be described, based on what have been reported in literature.  
6.1 Thermal decomposition of methyl chloride  
Only very few people have proposed a mechanism for the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride. Kondo 
et al. [42] proposed a very simple mechanism consisting of six reversible and six irreversible reactions. 
Weissman et al. [33] found the mechanism proposed by Kondo et al. [42] to be insufficient to understand the 
thermal decomposition of methyl chloride. Weissman et al. [33] therefore proposed a new mechanism based 
on their own experiments. Granada et al. [36] proposed a mechanism similar to that of Weissman et al. [33]. 
A combination of these two mechanisms will be presented.    
The decomposition of methyl chloride is initiated by its unimolecular decomposition into methyl and 
chlorine radicals. 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙(+𝑀) ⇄𝐶𝐻3 + 𝐶𝑙(+𝑀) 
R1b 
The chlorine radical does hereafter react with the methyl chloride to form chloromethyl and hydrogen 
chloride. 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝑙⇄𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻𝐶𝑙 
R9 
Once the hydrogen chloride is formed, it quickly reacts with the methyl radical, formed in R1b, to form 
methane which is an inevitable byproduct of the thermal degradation of methyl chloride as seen in the 
previous section. The chlorine radical is regenerated by this reaction and can thereby react with methyl 
chloride again [37]. 
 
𝐻𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻3 ⇄𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻4 
R646b 
The reaction R646b rapidly consumes methyl radicals and due to this chloromethyl is the most abundant C1 
radical in the system. The formed chloromethyl and methyl radicals can react with each other or recombine 
to form C2 hydrocarbons; however the recombination of methyl radicals only happens to a small extend 
because of its concentration [36]. 
 
𝐶𝐻3 + 𝐶𝐻3(+𝑀) ⇄𝐶2𝐻6(+𝑀) 
R940 
The recombination of chloromethyl radicals as well as the reaction between CH3 and CH2Cl, can form three 
different products. 
 
𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙⇄𝐶2𝐻4𝐶𝑙2 
R120 
 
𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙⇄𝐶2𝐻4𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝑙 
R121 
 
𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙⇄𝐶2𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻𝐶𝑙  
R122 
 
𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻3 ⇄𝐶2𝐻5𝐶𝑙   
R126 
 
𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻3  ⇄𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙 
R127 
 
𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻3  ⇄𝐶2𝐻5 + 𝐶𝑙 
R128 
The products from the recombination reactions may hereafter decompose into vinyl chloride, acetylene and 
ethylene [36]. 
==PAGE:31==
 
Page 29 of 111 
 
 
𝐶2𝐻4𝐶𝑙2 (+𝑀) ⇄𝐶2𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻𝐶𝑙 (+𝑀) 
R229 
 
𝐶2𝐻3𝐶𝑙 (+𝑀) ⇄𝐶2𝐻2 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙 (+𝑀) 
R189 
 
𝐶2𝐻5𝐶𝑙 (+𝑀) ⇄𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙 (+𝑀)  
R178 
 
𝐶2𝐻4𝐶𝑙 (+𝑀) ⇄𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐶𝑙 (+𝑀)  
R485 
Acetylene is primarily formed by the unimolecular decomposition of vinyl chloride in R189 [36][33]. The 
formation of ethylene have been reported by Granada et al. [36] to primarily occur via reaction R127 while it 
has been reported by Weissman et al. [33] to primarily occur through the decomposition of chloroethane 
R178. This difference might be because Weissman et al. [33] studied the decomposition of methyl chloride 
at higher pressures than Granada et al. [36].    
The formed ethylene and acetylene may react with radicals in which abstraction of hydrogen occurs. The 
fastest abstraction of hydrogen occurs with chlorine radicals [33] however the abstraction of hydrogen will 
also occur with chloromethyl radicals [36]. 
 
𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐶𝑙 ⇄𝐶2𝐻3 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙 
R649 
 
𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙 ⇄𝐶2𝐻3 + 𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙 
R130 
Similar reactions can be written for acetylene however these reactions are reported to be very slow and 
without a significant role [36] [33]. The formed C2H3 radicals react with acetylene and to a lesser extent with 
ethylene to form C4 radicals [36].  
 
𝐶2𝐻2 + 𝐶2𝐻3 ⇄𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝐻𝐶𝐻𝐶𝐻 
6.1 
 
𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐶2𝐻3 ⇄𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝐻𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝐻2 
6.2 
The reaction between the C2H3 radical and the acetylene results in the formation of a butadienyl radical. The 
formed butadienyl radical can subsequently undergo dehydrogenation and hydrogenation to form 
vinylacetylene and butadiene respectively. Furthermore, higher hydrocarbons may be formed by additional 
reaction with C2H3  or by the addition of butadienyl radical to acetylene [33]. 
The C2H3 radical may also decompose into acetylene instead of undergoing polymerization. 
 
𝐶2𝐻3 (+𝑀) ⇄𝐶2𝐻2 + 𝐻 (+𝑀) 
R992b 
The chlorine and the hydrogen radicals established in the radical pool may react with ethane, hydrogen and 
methyl chloride to form ethylene and hydrogen chloride respectively [33] [36]. 
 
𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶2𝐻6 ⇄𝐶2𝐻5 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙 
R647 
 
𝐶2𝐻5(+𝑀) ⇄𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐻(+𝑀) 
R950 
 
𝐻2 + 𝐶𝑙⇄𝐻+ 𝐻𝐶𝑙 
R659 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻⇄𝐶𝐻3 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙 
R3 
 
 
 
==PAGE:32==
 
Page 30 of 111 
 
The major reaction pathways for the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride are depicted in Figure 16. 
 
Figure 16: Major reaction pathway for the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride. 
Won [50] studied the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in a hydrogen atmosphere. The reaction 
pathway proposed by Won [50] is somewhat similar to the reaction pathway proposed by Granada et al. [36] 
and Weissman et al. [33]. However in the reaction pathway proposed by Won [50] the hydrogen radicals 
plays a more significant role as these are formed to a large extent by the reaction of chlorine radicals with 
hydrogen, R659.  
6.2 Oxidation of methyl chloride  
The major species produced doing the oxidation of methyl chloride are similar to those produced under the 
thermal decomposition of methyl chloride. However, the hydrocarbon is oxidized into various species under 
oxidative condition which does not occur doing the pure thermal decomposition. Furthermore, in the 
presence of oxygen, the C2H3 radicals are oxidized into CO which inhibits the polymerization reactions and 
thereby the formation of larger carbon species [36]. In this section the most important reactions for the 
oxidation of methyl chloride will be described based on what have been reported in [34] [36] [32] [37] [51] 
[52]. 
The decomposition of methyl chloride is initiated by its unimolecular decomposition into methyl and 
chlorine radicals and through the bimolecular reaction with oxygen to form chloromethyl and HO2. 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙(+𝑀) ⇄𝐶𝐻3 + 𝐶𝑙(+𝑀)  
R1b 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝑂2 ⇄𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻𝑂2 
R5 
As in the thermal decomposition the formed chlorine radical reacts with methyl chloride to form hydrogen 
chloride and chloromethyl. Upon the formation of hydrogen chloride, it rapidly converts the methyl radical, 
formed in R1b, to methane and regenerates the chlorine radical which can react with methyl chloride again 
[37].   
==PAGE:33==
 
Page 31 of 111 
 
   
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝑙⇄𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻𝐶𝑙  
 R9 
 
𝐻𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻3 ⇄𝐶𝐻4 + 𝐶𝑙  
R646b 
The presence of oxygen accelerates the conversion of methyl chloride as the formed hydrogen chloride may 
also react with hydroxide and thereby regenerate the chlorine radical [37]. 
 
𝑂𝐻+ 𝐻𝐶𝑙⇄𝐻2𝑂+ 𝐶𝑙  
R674 
Once the radical pool has been established, other radicals besides that of chlorine may react with methyl 
chloride at appreciable rates. The most important radical attacks on methyl chloride are given below. The 
radical attacks on methyl chloride are H abstraction reactions, except for reaction R3 [32]. 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻⇄𝐶𝐻3 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙  
R3 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻⇄𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻2 
R4 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝑂⇄𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝑂𝐻 
R6 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ +𝑂𝐻⇄𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻2𝑂   
R7 
 
𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻𝑂2 ⇄𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻2𝑂2  
R8 
Under fuel rich conditions, methyl chloride is primarily consumed by the reactions with hydrogen and 
chlorine radicals [51] while methyl chloride is primary consumed by the reactions with hydroxide and 
chlorine radicals under fuel lean condition [32][52]. This is because hydrogen radicals are less abundant than 
hydroxide radicals under lean fuel conditions [52].  
The formed chloromethyl may be oxidized to formaldehyde by reacting with oxygen.  
 
𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝑂2 ⇄ 𝐶𝐻2𝑂+ 𝐶𝑙𝑂 
R108 
The reaction between CH2Cl and O2 has been reported to be important as the modelling is very sensitive to 
this reaction [32], [34], [35], [52]. The reaction between CH2Cl and O2 does, however, only occur at a 
considerable rate at low temperatures while at high temperature the reaction does not contribute significantly 
to the consumption of chloromethyl radicals [32]. The consumption of chloromethyl radicals therefore 
mainly occurs by the recombination reactions [32][52]. Among these reactions the most important for the 
formation of ethylene and acetylene will be reviewed as all the recombination reactions were already shown 
in the previous section. The reaction between the methyl and choromethyl radicals is the primary pathway 
for the formation of ethylene [32] [36] [51] while the recombination of choromethyl radicals, is a major 
pathway for the formation of acetylene as the formed vinyl chloride, from this reaction, decomposes into 
acetylene [36].  
 
𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻3 ⇄𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙 
R127 
 
𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙+ 𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙⇄𝐶2𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ 𝐻𝐶𝑙  
R122 
 
𝐶2𝐻3𝐶𝑙+ (𝑀) ⇄𝐶2𝐻2 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙+ (𝑀) 
R188 
The formed acetylene is primarily consumed by the reactions with O and OH however the concentration of 
these two radicals are very low during the oxidation and due to this the consumption of acetylene is relative 
unimportant [37]. The formed ethylene is primary consumed by the reaction with chlorine, hydrogen and 
chloromethyl radicals which leads to the formation of C2H3 radicals [36] [37] [51]. 
 
𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐶𝑙⇄𝐶2𝐻3 + 𝐻𝐶𝑙  
R649 
==PAGE:34==
 
Page 32 of 111 
 
 
𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐻⇄𝐶2𝐻3 + 𝐻2 
R968 
 
𝐶2𝐻4 + 𝐶𝐻2𝐶𝑙⇄𝐶2𝐻3 + 𝐶𝐻3𝐶𝑙  
R130 
The reactions that consume ethylene are responsible for the majority of the C2H3 formed in the system. C2H3 
is one of the most important C2 radicals in the system as it responsible for the formation of larger 
hydrocarbons, as discussed in the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride. In addition the C2H3 radical is 
one of main precursor for the formation of acetylene through its unimolecular decomposition and with its 
reaction with oxygen [51]. 
 
𝐶2𝐻3(+𝑀) ⇄𝐶2𝐻2 + 𝐻(+𝑀)  
R992b 
 
𝐶2𝐻3 + 𝑂2 ⇄𝐶2𝐻2 + 𝐻𝑂2 
R1005 
Furthermore the oxidation of hydrocarbons primary occurs through the oxidation of C2H3 radicals. The 
formed C2H3 radicals can be oxidized by its reaction with oxygen to form formaldehyde and CHO radicals. 
This reaction is reported to be the main pathway for the consumption of oxygen together with the branching 
reaction, R725 [37][51].  
 
𝐶2𝐻3 + 𝑂2 ⇄𝐶𝐻2𝑂+ 𝐶𝐻𝑂  
R1009 
The CHO radical can be converted into CO by colliding with a third body or by reacting with oxygen 
however the formation of CO primary occurs due to the collision with a third body [37][51]. 
 
𝐶𝐻𝑂(+𝑀) ⇄𝐶𝑂+ 𝐻(+𝑀 ) 
R785 
 
𝐶𝐻𝑂+ 𝑂2 ⇄𝐶𝑂+ 𝐻𝑂2 
R790 
Formaldehyde can rapidly be converted into CHO by reacting with a chlorine radical and in this manner it 
can also be converted into CO [37]. Formaldehyde may also react with other radicals such as H, O, OH and 
HO2 in order to form CHO [32]. 
 
𝐶𝐻2𝑂+ 𝐶𝑙⇄𝐶𝐻𝑂+ 𝐻𝐶𝑙  
R668 
The oxidation of CO to CO2 is inhibited by the presence of chlorinated species in the reaction mixture. This 
occurs due to chlorine scavenging of hydrogen radicals which mainly occurs through the reactions in R3 and 
R659b [53][36]. 
 
𝐻+ 𝐻𝐶𝑙= 𝐻2 + 𝐶𝑙  
R659b 
These reactions are fast and thus efficiently scavenges the hydrogen radicals and contributes to the inhibition 
of the important chain branching given in R725. Due to this the concentration of hydroxide and O radicals 
are low in the system. 
 
𝐻+ 𝑂2 ⇄𝑂𝐻+ 𝑂  
R725 
Thus, the CO oxidation that proceed via the reaction with hydroxide is inherently slow[53].  
 
𝐶𝑂+ 𝑂𝐻⇄𝐶𝑂2 + 𝐻 
R753 
In addition to this, the reaction of hydroxide with hydrogen chloride, R674, is faster than its reaction with 
CO, and thus this reaction depletes the hydroxide radicals and limits the oxidation of CO through the 
reaction in R753 [34]. Consequently, the reaction between CO and HO2 becomes important in the oxidation 
of CO into CO2 [34][51]. 
==PAGE:35==
 
Page 33 of 111 
 
 
𝐶𝑂+ 𝐻𝑂2 ⇄𝐶𝑂2 + 𝑂𝐻 
R755 
The major pathways for the oxidization of methyl chloride are summarized schematically in Figure 17. In the 
figure the thickness of the lines are varied to illustrate the relative rate of the reactions.  
 
Figure 17: Major reaction pathways for the oxidation of methyl chloride [37]. 
7 Chemical kinetic model  
A chemical kinetic model for the decomposition of methyl chloride has been established. The chemical 
kinetic model consists of a methane mechanism, a HCl/Cl2 subset and a subset for chlorinated hydrocarbons. 
The methane mechanism was adopted from the work by Hashemi et al. [54]. The HCl/Cl2 subset was adopted 
from the work of Pelucchi et al. [55]. Both the methane mechanism and the HCl/Cl2 subset have been 
validated over a broad range of conditions and will not be further discussed here. For the subset of  the 
chlorinated hydrocarbons, the recent work by the Porro [56] has been adopted. The subset developed by 
Porro is mostly based on the work of Bozzelli et al. [34][57]. Besides using the work of Bozzelli, Porro 
included additional reactions in his subset. These additional reactions are also included in the present study 
in order to identify if potential important reactions may have been overlooked in the mechanism by Bozzelli 
et al. The additional reactions that Porro included were mostly irreversible reactions and the rate coefficients 
of these were estimated by himself. The reliability of these rate constants are questionable as it is not clear 
how they originated. 
In the mechanism published by Bozzelli et al. [34][57] there were several typographical errors which have 
been corrected in the mechanism published by [35][39][58] as these have had private communication with 
Bozzelli. These corrections are included in the chemical kinetic model established here. The work by 
Bozzelli et al. [34][57] was published in 1992; however several other groups have subsequently been 
interested in the decomposition of methyl chloride. Bozzelli has therefore provided updated rate coefficients 
for some of the reactions concerning the decomposition of methyl chloride to other studies. The updated rate 
constants are presented in work by Wang et al. [39] and Leylegian et al. [58]. These updated rate constants 
are used in the established kinetic mechanism instead of the original rate constant calculated by Bozzelli et al 
[34][57].

In this study the subset of the chlorinated hydrocarbons has been reviewed and more accurate rate 
coefficients have been implemented whenever these were available. This implies that the rate coefficients 
used in this mechanism have been based upon experimental measurements rather than theoretical rate 
constant. It should be noted that many of the rate coefficients from the work of Bozzelli et al. [34][57] are 
based upon theoretical calculations. Rate coefficients that are based on experimental measurements are 
preferred over purely theoretical coefficients as these are more accurate. However, the experimental rate 
coefficients should be determined within the temperature range of interest, as rate coefficients determined at 
low temperature may not extrapolate very well to high temperature. In the case that rate coefficients only 
have been determined experimentally in a low temperature range, theoretical coefficients are preferred, if 
these are valid in the temperature range of interest. For some reactions the rate coefficients have been 
determined experimentally by more than one group. In general coefficients that are based on recent studies 
are preferred over old ones as these are found more reliable. Typically, because new articles include old 
measurements or argue that the uncertainties of these are very large. However coefficients that are based 
upon a review which includes both old and new studies are highly preferred as these have showed to be 
robust over a wider range of conditions. 
==PAGE:40==
 
Page 38 of 111 
 
In the present work the thermodynamic data were taken from Hashemi et al. [54] for C-H-O species and 
from Porro [56] for chlorine containing species. The thermodynamic properties for the chlorine containing 
species are given in Appendix 13.2. 
8 Results and discussion 
The chemical kinetic model established is evaluated against some of the experimental data presented in 
section 5. The model is evaluated against flow reactor results, ignition delay times and burning velocity. It 
was chosen not to evaluate the model against experimental data on “chemical structure of premixed flat 
flames” as these data was obtained at atmospheric pressure which results in a very short flame and thereby 
large uncertainties.  
In the following section, experimental data and predictions of the model will be presented. All the 
simulations, presented in the following section, were performed in CHEMKIN [76].  
8.1 Flow reactor 
Of the flow reactor studies presented in section 5.1, it was chosen to validate the reaction mechanism against 
the experimental data determined by Won et al. [31], Hung et al. [32], Bozzelli et al. [34] and Roesler et al. 
[35]. All the experiments are modelled as an isothermal, isobaric batch reactor except for that of Roesler et 
al. which is modeled as an adiabatic, isobaric batch reactor.  
To identify which reactions the model predictions are sensitive to, sensitivity analysis will be performed 
together with the predictions. The sensitivity coefficient, SX,i, for the predicted concentration of species ‘X’ 
toward reaction ‘i’ is calculated as in Eq. 8.1. 
 
𝑆𝑋,𝑖= Δ[𝑋]/𝑋
Δ𝑘𝑖/𝑘𝑖
 
8.1 
A positive coefficient indicates that the reaction has a promoting effect on the concentration of the particular 
species.  
8.1.1 Thermal decomposition of methyl chloride 
In this section the model prediction will be evaluated against the experimental data on thermal 
decomposition of methyl chloride. 
8.1.1.1 Thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in nitrogen atmosphere  
A study of the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in a nitrogen atmosphere was conducted by Hung et 
al. [32]. The study was performed in a isothermal flow reactor in which the pressure was 1.14 atm and the 
temperature was varied from 1000 to 1350 K. In Figure 18 the model predictions and the experimental data 
of Hung et al. [32] are depicted.  
==PAGE:41==
 
Page 39 of 111 
 
 
 
Figure 18: Comparison of model predictions (lines) and experimental data (symbols) for thermal decomposition of methyl 
chloride (5100 ppm) in a nitrogen atmosphere plotted as a function of temperature. The pressure was 1.14 atm within the 
reactor and the residence time is calculated as 𝛕= 𝟐𝟏𝟓𝟏𝟎/𝐓. The experimental results are from Hung et al. [32]. 
The model predictions are in very good agreement with the experimental data however the model slightly 
over-predicts the concentration of ethylene. The prediction of ethylene does however show the same trend as 
the experimental data. 
To identify reactions for which the prediction of methyl chloride is sensitive to, a sensitive analysis is 
conducted. The sensitivity analysis is displayed in Figure 19.

Figure 19: Sensitivity for the prediction of methyl chloride under the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in a nitrogen 
atmosphere at 1333 K. The residence time was adjusted to achieve 20 % conversion of methyl chloride. 
The prediction of methyl chloride is most sensitive to the unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride, 
R1b, as the decomposition is initiated by this reaction. Furthermore, the unimolecular decomposition of 
methyl chloride is the only branching reaction present and thereby it is very important for the amount of 
radicals formed in the system. Thus, the amount of radical in the system is determined by this reaction and 
the terminating reactions. In the present study, the rate constant for the unimolecular decomposition is based 
on the work of Klippenstein et al. [59][60]. In their work the rate constant is theoretical calculated, however 
it has been validated against experimental data of Abadzhev et al. [77] and Lim and Michael [78]. The 
predictions of the rate constants are in good agreement with the experimental data of these two studies. 
Under the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride, the majority of methyl chloride is converted by 
reaction R9, CH3Cl+Cl=CH2Cl+HCl. Even though, the prediction of methyl chloride is not sensitive to this 
reaction. However the reactions consuming the chloromethyl radicals, formed from R9, are important for the 
prediction of methyl chloride as these reactions are pivotal for the radical pool in the system. The 
chloromethyl radicals are mainly consumed by the CH3/CH2Cl and CH2Cl/CH2Cl combination reactions 
which also have the largest sensitivity coefficients of the reactions consuming chloromethyl. The reaction 
CH2Cl+CH2Cl=CH2ClCH2+Cl speeds up the decomposition as it is a propagating reactions which forms 
radicals that are more reactive that the chloromethyl radicals. The chlorine radical formed from this reaction 
may react with methyl chloride again. The other CH3/CH2Cl and CH2Cl/CH2Cl combination reactions slow 
down the decomposition of methyl chloride as these are terminating reactions and thereby remove radicals 
from the system. The competition between these terminating and propagating reactions are therefore 
important for the radical pool in the system and thus also the prediction.  
Currently no experimental measurements of the rate constants for the CH3/CH2Cl and CH2Cl/CH2Cl 
combination reactions exist. In the present study, the rate constants of these reactions are based on the 
theoretical calculation of Bozzelli et al [34]. As these rate constants are purely based on theoretical 
calculations the uncertainties of these is expected to be quite high. Even though the rate constants for the 
CH3/CH2Cl and CH2Cl/CH2Cl combination reactions have not been characterized very well, good agreement 
between the experimental data and the model predictions are obtained by using the rate constants estimated 
by Bozzelli et al. [34] Karra and Senkan [69] have also investigated the rate constants for the recombination

reactions of CH2Cl and CH3 radicals theoretically. However, the rate constants proposed by Bozzelli et al. 
were found more reliable than that proposed by Karra and Senkan. 
From the sensitive analysis in Figure 19 it can also be seen that the prediction of methyl chloride is sensitive 
to the reaction CH3+HCl=Cl+CH4. This is propagating reaction in which the more reactive chlorine radical is 
reformed from the reaction between hydrogen chloride, formed in R9, with methyl chloride, formed in R1b. 
The chlorine radical formed from this reaction may react with methyl chloride again.

The prediction shows good agreement with the experimental data for methyl chloride and methane. However 
the model over-predicts the concentration of hydrogen chloride and under-predicts the concentration of 
ethane and ethylene. The over-prediction of hydrogen chloride may be because it is difficult to measure the 
concentration of hydrogen chloride as it may adsorb on the walls in the reactor. This statement is backed up 
by the fact that the chlorine balance based on the experimental data does not add up. However, it could also 
be because other chlorine species are formed which the model does not take into account. This is however 
unlikely as it was seen in section 5, that all methyl chloride was converted into hydrogen chloride for the 
experiments performed by  Bozzelli et al. [34] 
Under these conditions, where excess of hydrogen is present, the prediction of methyl chloride is still very 
sensitive to the unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride, which was also seen for the experiments of 
Hung et al. [32]. However, the prediction of methyl chloride also becomes very sensitive to reaction R3, 
CH3Cl+H=CH3+HCl, under these conditions. This can be seen from the sensitivity analysis presented in 
Figure 21. 
 
Figure 21: Sensitivity for the prediction of methyl chloride under the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in a hydrogen 
atmosphere at 1123K. The residence time was adjusted to achieve 20 % conversion of methyl chloride. 
The prediction of methyl chloride is very sensitive to the unimolecular decomposition as it is this reaction 
that initiates the decomposition of methyl chloride by forming methyl and chlorine radicals. These radicals 
react with molecular hydrogen and form hydrogen radicals. The formed hydrogen radicals react with methyl 
chloride, R3, and forms methyl radicals and hydrogen chloride. The methyl radical formed from reaction R3 
reacts with molecular hydrogen and reforms the hydrogen radical which can then react with methyl chloride 
again.  
 
𝐶𝐻3 + 𝐻2 = 𝐻+ 𝐶𝐻4  
R794b 
Due to this the prediction of methyl chloride is sensitive to reaction R3 and R794 when excess of hydrogen is 
present. The majority of the methyl chloride is consumed by reaction R3 under these conditions. However, a 
small fraction of the methyl chloride is converted into chloromethyl by reaction R4 and R9. Due to this the 
prediction of methyl chloride are also sensitive to the CH3/CH2Cl and CH2Cl/CH2Cl combination reactions 
as they are important  terminating reactions as discussed in the previous section.

8.1.2 Oxidation of methyl chloride  
In this section the model prediction will be evaluated against the experimental data on oxidation of methyl 
chloride. 
8.1.2.1 Oxidation of CH3Cl in Ar/H2/O2 mixtures  
Bozzelli et al. [34] studied the oxidation of methyl chloride in hydrogen/oxygen/argon mixtures. They 
studied the oxidation of methyl chloride in a temperature range of 1098-1223 K and at a total pressure of 1 
atmosphere within an isothermal tubular flow reactor. In Figure 23 the predicted and measured profiles of 
methyl chloride are depicted.  
 
Figure 23: Comparison of experimental data (symbols) and model prediction (lines) for oxidation of methyl chloride in 
H2/O2/Ar mixtures at different temperatures. The initial concentrations were 1 % H2, 1 % O2, 2 % CH3Cl and 96 % Ar. The 
experiments were conducted at 1 atm and the experimental results are from Bozzelli et al. [34]. 
From Figure 23, it is seen that there is a good agreement between the prediction and the experimental data at 
high temperature however at low temperature (1098 K and 1123 K) the decomposition of methyl chloride 
occurs too fast. To investigate the causes for the disagreement between the model and the experimental data 
at low temperature, a sensitivity analysis has been carried in order to see if the sensitivity coefficients change 
significantly over the temperature range. The result is depicted in Figure 24. 
The prediction of methyl chloride is most sensitive to the unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride 
and the reaction of CH2Cl with O2 over the temperature range. However the prediction is much more 
sensitive to the reaction of CH2Cl with O2 at low temperature while the prediction is more sensitive to the 
unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride at high temperature. For the reaction of CH2Cl with O2, there 
are currently no experimental measurements of the reaction constant. The rate constant used in the present 
work is based on the theoretical calculation of Bozzelli et al. [34]. Due to this it is expected that the 
uncertainty of this rate constant is quite high. As the predictions are very sensitive to the reaction of CH2Cl 
with O2   at low temperature and the uncertainty of rate constant is quite high, this might be the reason for the 
poor agreement between the model predictions and the experimental data at low temperature.

low temperature. At high temperature most of the chloromethyl is consumed by the recombination of 
chloromethyl radicals and the reactions with methyl radicals. This can be seen from Figure 25.

From the sensitivity analysis, in Figure 24, it is also seen that the prediction of methyl chloride is also 
sensitive to the CH3/CH2Cl and CH2Cl/CH2Cl combination reactions under these conditions as they are 
important terminating and propagating reactions. Based on this and the previous results it may be concluded 
that the prediction of methyl chloride is most sensitive to the unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride 
as this reaction initiates the decomposition. Furthermore, the prediction of methyl chloride is sensitive to the 
reactions that mainly consume chloromethyl radicals as these are among the most important terminating and 
propagating reactions in the system.

8.1.2.2 Oxidation of methyl chloride under fuel lean conditions 
Hung et al. [32] studied the oxidation of methyl chloride under fuel lean condtions. They investigated the 
oxidation in the tempature range 800-1350 K and at a total pressure of 1.14 atm. In Figure 28, the 
experimental data is depicted together with the model predictions. 
 
Figure 28: Comparison of model predictions (lines) and experimental data (symbols) for oxidation of methyl chloride (4800 
ppm) in air plotted as a function of temperature. The pressure was 1.14 atm within the reactor and the residence time is 
calculated as 𝛕= 𝟐𝟏𝟓𝟏𝟎/𝐓. The experimental results are from Hung et al. [32]. 
 
From Figure 28 it can be seen that the oxidation of methyl chloride occurs too fast and due to this the 
prediction of the other species are off.  For the prediction, the oxidation of methyl chloride starts to occur at 
50 K below that of the experimental data. A sensitivity analysis is performed to investigate which reactions

Of the reactions that appear in the sensitivity analysis the reactions CH2Cl+HO2=CH2ClO+OH, 
CH2ClO=ClCHO+H, CH2ClO=CH2O+Cl and CH3Cl+HO2= CH2Cl+H2O2 have not been characterized very 
well in literature. The rate constants used for these reactions in the present work is based on the theoretical 
calculation of Bozzelli et al. [34]. As the prediction is very sensitive to these reactions and the uncertainty of 
these rate constants are quite high, this is likely the reason for the poor agreement between the model 
predictions and the experimental data under fuel lean conditions. 
From this is can be concluded that the model cannot be used to predict the oxidation of methyl chloride 
under very fuel lean conditions. This is not a problem for this study as there will be fuel rich conditions 
during gasification. However, it will of course be preferred that the model can be used under all conditions.  
8.1.2.3    Inhibition effect of methyl chloride on oxidation of CO 
Roesler et al. [35] studied the inhibition effect of methyl chloride on oxidation of carbon monoxide in an 
adiabatic flow reactor. From their study, Roesler et al. [35] found that the full oxidation process occurs in 
two steps. First, methyl chloride is consumed in the reaction gas consisting of CO, H2O and O2. The gas 
composition nearly remains unchanged until all methyl chloride have been consumed. As the methyl chloride 
is completely consumed the carbon monoxide is rapidly oxidized. This work is mainly focusing on the first 
step as the oxidation of carbon monoxide in the presents of hydrogen chloride have been studied by Pelucchi 
et al. [55].  
In the experiments by Roesler et al. [35] the time at which the reaction starts is ill defined as mixing and 
reactions occur simultaneously in the first part of the reactor. Due to this the measured concentration of each 
species at the first sample point is used as the initial conditions for the model. In order to fit the model results 
to the experimental data it is necessary to shift the model results to an arbitrary degree of conversion of 
methyl chloride. In the present work the model results have been shifted to match the point of 50 %

curves have a similar shape to that of the experimental data except for formaldehyde for mixture A. As the 
concentrations of these species are in the ppm range the uncertainty is expected to be rather large and 
therefore no further quantitatively conclusion will be made based on these.

Under these conditions, the prediction of methyl chloride is very sensitive to the reactions H+O2=O+OH and 
CO+OH=CO2+H for both mixtures. Both of these reactions have a large negative sensitivity coefficient as 
they are important for the amount of radicals formed. This branching and propagating reaction form a cycle 
in which the net production is an O radical. The formation of radicals speeds up the conversion of methyl 
chloride and therefore these reactions have negative coefficients. From the sensitive analysis it can also be 
seen that the other reactions consuming H and OH radicals have positive sensitivity coefficients as these 
compete for the H and OH radicals with the branching and propagating reaction. Due to this it can be seen 
that the reactions of methyl chloride with H and OH radicals have relative large positive sensitivity 
coefficients. Similar, the reaction H+O2(+M)=HO2+(M) has a large positive sensitive coefficient for mixture 
A as this reaction is a terminating reaction. The reaction OH+OH=O+H2O occurs in the reverse direction and 
does thereby form a branching reaction. Due to this, the reaction has a negative sensitivity coefficient.  
In comparison with the predictions of the previous experimental data, this system is not very sensitive to the 
combination reactions of CH3/CH2Cl and CH2Cl/CH2Cl and the unimolecular decomposition of methyl 
chloride. The reason for this is that the gas composition is significantly different. In the experiments of 
Roesler et al. [35] methyl chloride is a minor component in the gas while it is a major component in the other 
experiments presented. Due to this different branching and terminating reactions becomes important. In the 
experiments of Roesler et al. [35] the majority of the radicals are produced by the reaction H+O2=O+OH 
while the majority of the radicals were produced from the unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride in 
the other experiments.

Validation of a detailed reaction mechanism is often carried out by comparing calculated ignition delays to 
shock tube experiments. Ignition delay can be defined in various ways and thereby there is not a fixed

definition. Ignition delay may be defined as the time at which either the maximum or onset of certain species

Ignition delays for mixtures of methyl chloride have been experimental measured by Miller et al. [39] and 
Shin et al. [40]. These experimental data will be compared with the model predictions. During the prediction 
of the ignition delays sensitivity analysis will be conducted. The sensitivity coefficients are calculated as in

A positive coefficient indicates that the reaction prolongs the ignition delay while a negative coefficient 
indicates that the reaction reduces the ignition delay. The prediction of ignition delays is carried out as an 
adiabatic batch reactor with constant volume.  
8.2.1 Ignition delay for methyl chloride 
Miller et al. [39] measured the ignition delay of methyl chloride and methane mixtures at a pressure of 2 atm 
and temperatures of 1300-1600 K. The composition of the mixtures studied by Miller et al. [39] is given in 
Table 8. Miller et al. [48] defined the ignition delay time as the interval between the arrival of the shock and 
a sudden increase in temperature due to the exothermic reactions taking place. The ignition delay is thus 
simulated as the time where the slope of the temperature profile reaches its maximum value.

The model is able to predict the ignition delay times for pure methane very well while the ignition for the 
mixtures containing methyl chloride occurs too fast. The deviation between the prediction and the 
experimental data is around 45 % for mixture 1 and 3. To identity the cause for the difference between the 
model and the experimental data a sensitive analysis is conducted. The sensitivity analysis is depicted in 
Figure 34. 
 
Figure 34: Sensitivity of ignition delay times for mixtures containing methyl chloride at 1460 K. 
The prediction is very sensitive to the unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride. The rate constant of 
this reaction has been validated over a wide range of conditions by Klippenstein et al. [60][59] and therefore 
thought to be reliable. The prediction of the ignition delays are however, also very sensitive to the reaction of 
CH2Cl with O2 which was shown to cause problems in predicting the oxidation of methyl chloride for the 
experimental data of Bozzelli et al. [34]. This reaction may also be the cause for the slight under-prediction 
of the ignition delay times. To improve the model prediction this reaction should be studied in more details 
through experimental studies or at a high level of theory as the prediction of the model is sensitive to this 
reaction under some conditions.  
The recombination reactions of chloromethyl radical may also be the reason for the poor agreement as these 
are not characterized well in literature and the prediction is also very sensitive to these reactions. However, 
these reactions have not caused problems in the predictions of the previous experiments.

as the time at which the maximum OH concentration is reached. The composition of the mixtures 
investigated by Shin et al. [40] can be seen in Table 9.

The predictions and the experimental data of the ignition delay times are depicted in Figure 35.  
 
Figure 35: Ignition delay times for mixtures given in Table 9. Symbols mark experimental results from Shin et al. [40] and 
lines denotes the prediction of the present model. The simulations are conducted at a fixed pressure of 1.22 bar while the

The model is not able to predict the ignition delay for the mixture only containing methane (Mixture 1) very 
well while the agreement with the experimental data increase with the loading of methyl chloride within the 
mixtures. The slopes of the predicted curves are not as steep as those of the experimental data. The reason for 
this is probably because that the methane mechanism is not able to predict the ignition delay for the mixture 
containing only methane and thereby also influence the prediction of the ignition delay for mixture 2 and 3. 
Methyl chloride is only presented in very small quantities compared to methane in mixture 2 and 3 and due 
to this the prediction of these mixtures are largely influenced by the methane mechanism. The methane 
mechanism has however previously been used to predict ignitions delay times for methane-air/O2 mixtures 
with good accuracy [54]. The poor agreement between the prediction and the experimental data might also 
be because an average pressure, instead of the exact pressure, is used to predict the ignition delay.  
The model is however able to predict the promoting effect that methyl chloride has on the ignition of 
methane as the ignition delay time decreases with increasing concentration of methyl chloride.  
8.3 Laminar Burning velocity  
The laminar burning velocity is as ignition delay often used to validate detailed reaction mechanisms. To 
simulate laminar burning velocities are however different from both flow reactor and ignition delay 
experiments as it in addition to the kinetic mechanism requires transport parameters for each of the chemical 
species. In the present work the transport parameters were taken from Hashemi et al. [54] for  C-H-O species 
and from the work of Porro [56] for Cl-containing species. The transport parameters for the chlorinate

8.3.1 Burning velocity of CH3Cl and CH3Cl/CH4 in air 
The prediction for burning velocity for pure methyl chloride–air flames are compared with experimental data 
in Figure 36. There is a significant great difference between the experimental data obtained by the two 
groups however the experimental data show the same trend and the peak occurs at about the same 
equivalence ratio.  
 
Figure 36: Laminar burning velocity of pure methyl chloride–air mixtures as a function of equivalence ratio. The circles are 
experimental results from Kaesche-Krischer [45] while the squares are from Chelliah et al. [45]. The line denotes the model 
prediction. The initial temperature was 298 K while the pressure was 1 atm. 
The model under-predicts the burning velocity however the agreement between the prediction and the 
experimental data seems to increase with increasing equivalence ratio. The model predictions are thus better 
at fuel-rich conditions which were also observed for the flow reactor experiments. To identify the causes for 
the poor agreement between the model and experiments at fuel lean conditions the sensitivity for the mass 
flow rate is calculated. As the conditions at the inlet of the system is fixed, the sensitivity of the mass flow 
rate is linearly related to the sensitivity of the burning velocity [54].  
From the sensitivity analysis in Figure 37, it can be seen that there is a large shift in the sensitivity of the 
different reactions over the equivalence ratio investigated. At fuel lean conditions the prediction is very 
sensitive to the reactions involving chloromethyl while it is more sensitive to the reactions H+O2=O+OH and 
HCO(+M)=H+CO(+M) at fuel rich conditions. The rate constants for the reactions involving chloromethyl 
presented in the sensitivity analysis are based on the theoretical work of Bozzelli et al. [34]. The rate 
constants calculated by Bozzeli et al. [34] were only intended to be used within the temperature range 973-
1223 K. The temperatures within the flames greatly exceed this (1700-2000 K), and this might be the reason 
for the poor agreement at fuel lean conditions where the prediction is very sensitive to these reactions. Thus, 
the rate constants of these reactions may not extrapolate very well to high temperatures. Due to this it is

The prediction lies in between the two sets of data for most of the equivalence ratios. The method used in the 
study of Valeiras et al. [44] is known to underestimate the burning velocity while the method by Chelliah et 
al. [45] overestimates the burning velocity with 5-10 %. Taken this into account the prediction is in good 
agreement with the experimental data. In contrast to the predictions for pure methyl chloride–air flames, the 
model is able to make good predictions at fuel lean conditions for fuels consisting of equal amount methyl 
chloride and methane. To identify why the model is able to give a good prediction of the burning velocities 
for fuels containing equal amounts of methyl chloride and methane at fuel lean conditions a sensitivity 
analysis is performed, Figure 39. 
 
Figure 39: Sensitivity of the mass flow rate for CH3Cl/CH4/air mixtures with R=1 at different equivalence ratios. 
The prediction of the burning velocity, for fuels containing equal amounts of methyl chloride and methane, is 
very sensitive to the reactions in the methane mechanism and only slightly sensitive to the reactions 
involving chlorinated species. The prediction even becomes less sensitive to the reaction involving Cl-
containing species with increasing equivalence ratio. This was also observed by Wang et al. [39]. As the 
reactions in the methane mechanism are well characterized, the model is able to predict the burning 
velocities for fuels contain equal amounts of methyl chloride and methane. Typically when prediction 
burning velocities the chain branching reaction H+O2=O+OH is found to be the most important reaction 
[54], which is also seen here for R=1. The unimolecular decomposition of HCO promotes the burning 
velocity whereas it reactions with Cl and O2 slows down the burning velocity as these reactions terminate the 
radical chain. The unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride is seen to slow down the burning velocity 
as this reactions runs in the reverse direction at these conditions and functions at a terminating reaction. 
Furthermore, the prediction is seen to be very sensitivity to the oxidation of CO to CO2 at small equivalence 
ratios.

Figure 40: Burning velocity for stoichiometric mixtures of CH3Cl/CH4/air as a function of the mole present of methyl chloride

From Figure 40 it is seen that the model systematically under-predicts the burning velocity by 5 cm/s in the 
entire range of the fuel composition. The prediction does however follow the same trend as the experimental 
data. If 5 cm/s were added to the predictions of the burning velocity there will be a perfect agreement 
between the experimental data and the predictions. This systematic deviation between the prediction and the 
experimental data can be attributed to the fact that the method used by Chelliah et al. [45] overestimates the 
burning velocity. In Figure 38 the experimental data of Chelliah et al. [45] was also seen to be 3-4 m/s above 
the predictions. Thus, this seems as a reasonable explanation.   
8.3.2 Flame speed of CH3Cl/CH4 under oxygen enrichment 
Shin et al. [46] studied the flame speed of CH3Cl/CH4 under oxygen enrichment conditions. In Figure 41 the 
experimental data is depicted together with the predictions of the model. From Figure 41 it is seen that the 
model under-predicts the burning velocity by approximately 20 % except for R=0 Ω=0.3 where the 
prediction is almost perfect. In general it can be seen that the model is able to predict the same trend as 
shown by the experimental data. Again, it is observed that that the agreement between the experimental data 
and prediction increases with increasing equivalence ratios for the mixtures containing methyl chloride. 
Thus, the model performs best under fuel rich conditions which is good as there will be fuel rich conditions 
under gasification.

8.4 Reduced mechanism  
In the subset developed “all” possible reactions between “all” the species in the system have been considered 
by including the irreversible reactions study by Porro [56] in the starting mechanism. None of these reactions 
were important as they did not appear in any of the sensitivity analysis. Furthermore, none of these reactions 
appeared as a major reaction pathway in the reaction pathway analysis performed. Due to this the subset for 
the chlorinated hydrocarbons can be reduced to the reactions displayed in Table 7. The simulations 
performed above were repeated with the reduced mechanism and almost identical predictions were obtained. 
However the prediction on the thermal conversion of methyl chloride will be performed with the full subset 
as some of these reactions may be important under gasification conditions.  
8.5 Sub-conclusion 
The established chemical kinetic model gave satisfactory predictions of the ignition delay, burning velocity 
and the product distribution in the flow reactor studies for mixtures containing methyl chloride. However 
improvements to the mechanism can still be achieved. For the flow reactor studies, it was seen that the 
decomposition of methyl chloride occurred to fast at low temperatures in the present of oxygen. Similar, in 
the prediction of the ignition delay, the ignition occurred too fast. The reason for both of these observation 
were attributed to the reaction of CH2Cl with O2 as the predictions were very sensitive to this reaction and the 
uncertainty of this rate constant is rather large as they are based on theoretical work of Bozzelli et al. [34]. It 
is expected that the predictions of the model can be improved by studying this reaction in more details. For 
both of these kinds of experiments the unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride was shown to be the 
most important reaction as it initiates the decomposition of methyl chloride and the formation of radicals. 
The chemical kinetic model slightly under-predicted the burning velocity of the CH3Cl/CH4/air flames 
however the prediction reproduced the same trend as the experimental data. The model was not able to 
predict the burning velocity of pure methyl chloride –air flames with very good accuracy at low equivalence 
ratio as the prediction at these conditions is very sensitive to the reactions with chloromethyl. It is expected 
that the prediction can be improved by studying the reactions involving chloromethyl in more details.

The kinetic model has been validated over a temperature range representative for gasification. However, the 
validation has been carried out with gas compositions which are not very representative for gasification 
except that of Roesler et al. [35]. In the major part of the experimental data methyl chloride is one of the 
major components in the gas which will not be the case under gasification conditions. Caution should 
therefore be taken when using the model to characterize the transformation of methyl chloride under 
gasification conditions. Nevertheless, the model had shown good agreement with the experimental data over 
a wide range of conditions, especially under reducing conditions. The model is therefore found suited to be 
used to predict the thermal conversion of methyl chloride under gasification conditions.  
9 Thermal conversion of methyl chloride under gasification conditions  
In this section the kinetic model developed will be used to model the thermal conversion of methyl chloride 
under gasification conditions. The conditions within a gasifier (temperature, gas composition etc.) are 
dependent on the gasification technology used and therefore simulations will be conducted for fixed, 
fluidized and entrained bed gasifiers. By modeling the thermal conversion of methyl chloride for each 
gasification technology, it is possible to identify in which technologies methyl chloride will appear in the exit 
gas and cause problems in the downstream process. In addition to this, the pathway for the transformation of 
methyl chloride under gasification conditions will be described in this section.  
The gasification conditions are, in addition to the gasification technology, also very dependent on the 
gasifying agent used. This is illustrated in Table 10 where the exit gas composition and operating 
temperature for an atmospheric bubbling fluidized bed is given for three different gasifying agents. Due to 
this, it is chosen to focus on atmospheric air blown gasifiers as these are most commonly used. 
Table 10: Gas composition from gasification of biomass in an atmospheric bubbling fluidized bed with three different 
gasifying agent [12]

9.1 General assumptions 
In order to model the thermal conversion of methyl chloride under gasification conditions, assumptions have 
to be made. The general assumptions made across gasification technologies will be described and discussed 
below while the assumptions specific for each gasification technology will be discussed later.   
In the literature on biomass gasification, there are plenty of studies reporting the exit gas composition, from 
gasifiers, as a function of the operating conditions. However only very few groups have studied the gas 
composition within gasification reactors and due to this only limited data on the gas composition inside 
gasifiers are available [80] [81]. Consequently, in the modeling of the thermal conversion of methyl chloride, 
the gas composition within the gasifier is assumed to be the same as the exit gas composition. This 
assumption seems reasonable for BFB’s as the variations within the gasifier have been reported to be similar 
to the exit gas composition [81]. Similarly this assumption seems reasonable for CFB, based on the axial gas 
concentration profiles measured by Kersten et al. [82] and Li et al. [83]. The axial concentration profiles for 
the study of Li et al. [83] are shown in Figure 42. For the other gasification technologies the accuracy of this 
assumption is unclear as no axial gas concentrations have been reported.  
==PAGE:64==
 
Page 62 of 111 
 
 
Figure 42: Measured axial gas composition profiles for CO2, CO, H2 and CH4 at the center of a CFB. The profiles for the 
closed and open points are for two different runs [83]. 
For each gasification technology the exit gas composition from several studies will be considered and the 
average composition will be used in the modelling. This is thought to make the predictions more 
representative as the gas composition reported in literature varies a lot even when the same gasification 
technology and gasifying agent are used. Generally, only the major components in the exit gas (H2, CO, CO2, 
CH4, C2H4, N2 and H2O) are reported in literature. Due to this it is assumed that the gas within the gasifier 
only consist of these species and methyl chloride. 
Unfortunately, only few groups have measured the concentration of methyl chloride in the product gas from 
gasifiers. At DTU Risø the concentration of methyl chloride in the product gas from a low-temperature 
circulating fluidized bed gasifier (650 °C) fueled with wheat straw has been measured. The product gas 
contained 90-100 ppm of methyl chloride which corresponds to 15 % of the chlorine present in the fuel [84]. 
Based on this, it is assumed that the gas contains 100 ppm of methyl chloride in the modelling. The 
concentration of methyl chloride may, however, be significantly lower for gasifiers using biomass with a low 
chlorine content such as wood biomass. Furthermore, the formation of methyl chloride is temperature 
dependent [22] so the formation may vary greatly dependent on the operating temperature of the gasifier.  
For all gasification technologies it is assumed that the gas flow can be approximated by plug flow throughout 
the gasifier. The decomposition of methyl chloride is furthermore assumed to be kinetically controlled and 
mixing limitations are thereby neglected in the modelling.  Furthermore, it is assumed that the zone, in which 
the thermal conversion of methyl chloride occurs, is isothermal. These assumptions will be discussed 
separately for each gasification technology. 
9.2 Circulating fluidized bed 
In this section the thermal conversion of methyl chloride in a CFB is modeled.  
9.2.1 Assumption  
In the CFB it is assumed that the drying, pyrolysis and combustion occur in the bottom of the gasifier while 
the thermal conversion of methyl chloride occurs in the dilute zone. This assumption implies that all methyl 
chloride is formed in bottom of the gasifier while the thermal conversion of it only occurs in the dilute zone. 
From the study of  Kersten et al. [85] it seems reasonable to assume that the pyrolysis and combustion occur 
in the bottom of the gasifier as they found that that the majority of the pyrolysis took place within the first 
1.5 meter of their 6 meter high gasifier. Furthermore, Kersten et al. [85] found that the combustion zone is 
located within the first 0.7 meters of gasifier as they never detected oxygen at their lowest sampling point 
==PAGE:65==
 
Page 63 of 111 
 
which was located 0.7 meters above the air nozzles. As all oxygen is rapidly consumed in the bottom of the 
gasifier the thermal conversion of methyl chloride in the dilute zone will be modelled in the absence of 
oxygen. 
In order to determine if methyl chloride will be present in the exit gas from CFB gasifiers, typically gas 
residence time within the dilute zone has to be estimated. Unfortunately, typical gas residence times within 
CFB gasifiers have not been reported in literature and therefore they are estimated based on the gas velocity 
at the outlet of gasifier and the height of gasifier. In the gasification experiment of [85] [82] the gas velocity 
has been reported to be 6-9 m/s and 3.7-5.7 m/s while the height of the gasifier was 6 and 6.5 meter 
respectively. Based on the study of Kersten et al. [85] it is assumed that the combustion and pyrolysis occur 
within the first 1.5 meter of the riser, and the residence time within the dilute zone thereby becomes 0.5-1.4 
seconds.  
In Table 11, the exit gas composition and the operating temperatures for several CFB biomass gasifiers are 
given. The average gas compositions reported in Table 11 is assumed to be a representative gas composition 
for CFB gasifiers.  Due to this the average gas composition is used in the modelling. 
Table 11: Exit gas composition from CFB biomass gasifiers. The gas composition is given on a dry basis in vol%.a Low-
temperature CFB gasifer at DTU Risø.

To make the modelling as simple as possible it is assumed that the dilute zone in the CFB gasifier is 
isothermal and that the gas flows like a plug. In the CFB gasifier, the axial temperature gradient in the riser is 
small and has been reported to be between 30 and 50 °C from the top to the bottom [82][84]. Due to this it is 
reasonable to model the CFB gasifier as isothermal. For the assumption about plug flow the situation is a 
little more complicated. In a CFB the solid biomass trend to migrate towards the wall which causes more gas 
to be produced near the wall. Due to this, parabolic radial gas profiles within the gasifier appear as illustrated 
in Figure 43. The assumption about plug flow may therefore not be so accurate for the CFB gasifier however 
it is the best assumption to make without using computational fluid dynamics.  
==PAGE:66==
 
Page 64 of 111 
 
 
Figure 43: Radial gas composition profiles within a CFB Gasifier [88]. 
9.2.2 Results  
The results for the thermal conversion of methyl chloride within a CFB are shown in Figure 44. To 
investigate the influence of temperature, simulations have been performed for temperatures within the 
temperature range reported in Table 11. 
 
Figure 44: Thermal conversion of methyl chloride within a CFB gasifier. The gas composition used in the simulation is the 
average value reported in Table 11. 
From Figure 44 it can be seen that the thermal conversion of methyl chloride is very sensitive to the 
temperature. At low temperatures (650-700 °C), little methyl chloride is converted. Consequently all the 
methyl chloride formed within gasifiers operating at low temperatures, will appear in the exit gas. This result 
is interesting as the low-temperature circulating fluidized bed gasifier at Risø, from which the initial 
concentration of methyl chloride was taken, operates at 650 °C. The simulation thereby justifies the use of 
this concentration as the initial concentration of methyl chloride.  
As the temperature increases above 700 °C, the conversion of methyl chloride increases. However even at 
the highest temperature of 950 °C and the longest residence time estimated, it is not possible to obtain a 
methyl chloride concentration less than 1 ppm which is required for the downstream process. Due to this it

seems very reasonable that a substantial amount of the methyl chloride formed within a CFB gasifier will 
appear in the exit gas. The conclusion will even remain the same if the gas residence time was significantly 
longer than the one estimated. Therefore the conclusion seems reliable.  
During the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride, the concentration of the major species remains 
constant when the temperature is kept below 800 °C. However as the temperature increases above 850 °C 
some of the ethylene is decomposed into hydrogen and acetylene. A plot of the major components is 
depicted in Figure 45 as a function of the residence time for a temperature of 950 °C.  
 
Figure 45: Predicted gas composition during the thermal conversion of methyl chloride with in a CFB gasifier at 950 °C.  The 
N2 mole fraction is depicted on the secondary y-axis to the right while the other components are depicted on the y-axis to the 
left. 
Despite the decomposition of ethylene, the gas composition is close to constant during the thermal 
conversion of methyl chloride. As a consequence of this it is possible to selectively remove methyl chloride 
by heating the produced gas in a thermal cycle.  
9.2.3 Reaction pathway 
The conversion of methyl chloride under gasification conditions is rather simple. The reaction pathway for 
the consumption of methyl chloride is depicted in Figure 46. Methyl chloride is mainly decomposed by the 
unimolecular decomposition and by reaction with atomic hydrogen. The majority of the methyl chloride is 
converted by the reaction with atomic hydrogen in which methyl radicals and hydrogen chloride are formed. 
As the temperature increases a larger fraction of the methyl chloride is converted by its unimolecular 
decomposition. The majority of the formed methyl radicals react with water, molecular hydrogen or atomic 
hydrogen to form methane.  
A part of the methyl chloride is converted by the reaction CH3Cl+H=CH2Cl+H2 which forms chloromethyl. 
However almost all the chloromethyl radicals are converted back into methyl chloride by the reaction with 
ethylene, CH2Cl+C2H4=>CH3Cl+C2H3. The chloromethyl that is not converted back into methyl chloride is 
converted into chlorine radicals and hydrogen chloride by reacting with atomic hydrogen and methyl radicals 
respectively. A small amount of chloroethane is formed from the reaction between chloromethyl and methyl; 
however, chloroethane is rapidly decomposed into ethylene and hydrogen chloride.

The chlorine formed, from the unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride and the reaction of 
chloromethyl with atomic hydrogen, reacts with molecular hydrogen, methane and ethylene to form 
hydrogen chloride. All the chlorine from methyl chloride is thereby converted into hydrogen chloride.  
 
Figure 46: Reaction pathway for the methyl chloride consumption under gasification conditions. 
The reaction pathway of methyl chloride under gasification conditions is very similar to the reaction pathway 
of methyl chloride in a hydrogen atmosphere which was discussed under the validation of the kinetic model. 
However under gasification conditions the prediction of methyl chloride is not sensitive to the reactions with 
chloromethyl, as was seen in the validation of the mechanism. This is because methyl chloride is not a major 
component in the gas under gasification conditions as it is in the validation experiment. Due to this only a 
very small amount of chloromethyl is formed under gasification conditions and the reactions consuming 
chloromethyl radicals do not become important terminating and propagating reactions in the system.  
9.2.4 Sensitivity  
A sensitive analysis for the prediction of methyl chloride is depicted in Figure 47.  
 
Figure 47: Sensitivity for the prediction of methyl chloride under the thermal decomposition of methyl chloride in a CFB 
gasifier. The residence time was adjusted to achieve 20 % conversion of methyl chloride.

The prediction of methyl chloride is most sensitive to the two reactions which mainly consume methyl 
chloride. The sensitivity analysis also indicates that the prediction of methyl chloride is sensitive to the 
reactions which consume and produce atomic hydrogen. Atomic hydrogen is produced from the reaction of 
C2H5 with molecular hydrogen and thereby increasing the rate of the decomposition of methyl chloride while 
atomic hydrogen is consumed by the reaction with methyl and thereby slows down the consumption of 
methyl chloride. The reactions that appear in the sensitivity analysis, Figure 47, have all been well 
characterized and due to this the predictions of methyl chloride seems reliable. 
To investigate how sensitive the thermal conversion of methyl chloride is to the gas composition, simulations 
in which the gas composition is changed are made. As the gas composition varies significantly from one 
study to another, it is decided to increase the concentration of each species with 50 % from the average value 
one at a time. When the concentration of one species is increased, the concentration of nitrogen is decreased. 
The result of this is plotted in Figure 48 for a fixed temperature of 850 °C.  
 
Figure 48: Thermal conversion of methyl chloride at a temperature of 850 °C and with varying gas composition. The 
concentration of each species is increased by 50 % from the average value reported in Table 11. 
From Figure 48, it is seen that an increase in the hydrogen content, increases the decomposition of methyl 
chloride. By increasing the hydrogen content more hydrogen radicals are formed and this increased the 
decomposition of methyl chloride as the majority of the methyl chloride is consumed by the reaction with 
hydrogen radicals. By increasing the concentration of water and ethylene the decomposition of methyl 
chloride occurs slightly faster. This is due to an increase in the radicals formed when the concentration of 
these two species is increased. The increase in the methane concentration slows down the decomposition of 
methyl chloride. This might be because the majority of methyl chloride is converted into methane and by 
increasing the methane content of the gas the equilibrium is switched and the conversion is slowed down. 
The decomposition of methyl chloride is not affected by the increase in CO and CO2 concentration. 
Simulations similar to those in Figure 48 have also been conducted for temperatures between 700 and 950 
°C. Similar trends as those shown in Figure 48 were observed. From this it can be concluded that the thermal 
conversion of methyl chloride is not very sensitive to the gas composition compared to how sensitive it is to 
the operating temperature of the gasifier. The thermal conversion of methyl chloride is thereby mostly 
dependent on the temperature within the gasifier.

In order to investigate how sensitive the thermal conversion of methyl chloride is to the initial concentration 
of methyl chloride chosen, simulations with different initial concentrations of methyl chloride were 
conducted. The results are depicted in Figure 49. 
 
Figure 49: Thermal conversion of methyl chloride at a temperature of 850 °C and with varying initial concentration of 
methyl chloride. The gas composition used in the simulation is the average value reported in Table 11. 
The conversion degree of methyl chloride is only slightly influenced by the initial concentration of methyl 
chloride. Thus, the time to obtain full conversion of methyl chloride is almost independent of the initial 
concentration. However, the degree of conversion required to achieve a concentration of methyl chloride 
under 1 ppm decreases with the initial concentration. For an initial concentration of 25 ppm a conversion 
degree larger than 96 % is only required. Such high degrees of conversion are only achieved at the highest 
temperature of 950 °C and the longest residence time in a CFB. Due to this it seems very reasonable that a 
substantial amount of the methyl chloride formed within a CFB gasifier will appear in the exit gas even if the 
initial concentration of methyl chloride was significantly lower than the one used in the simulations. 
9.3 Bubbling fluidized bed  
In this section the thermal conversion of methyl chloride in a BFB gasifier is modeled. 
9.3.1 Assumption 
In the BFB it is assumed that the drying, pyrolysis and combustion occur in the bed while the thermal 
conversion of methyl chloride occurs in the freeboard. The thermal conversion of methyl chloride is 
modelled in the absence of oxygen as it has been reported by Ross et al. [81] that all oxygen is rapidly 
consumed in the bed. Ross et al. [81] studied the axial gas profiles in a 2.7 m high BFB biomass gasifier and 
detected no oxygen beyond their second sampling point which was located 12 cm above the air distributor 
plate. 
In Table 12, the exit gas composition and the operating temperatures for several BFB biomass gasifiers are 
given. The average gas compositions reported in Table 12 is assumed to be a representative gas composition 
for BFB gasifiers.  Due to this the average gas composition is used in the modelling. By comparing Table 11 
and Table 12 it can be seen that the BFB and the CFB operate within the same temperature range and that the 
exit gas composition of the two gasifiers are similar.

In the freeboard of a BFB, plug flow gives a reasonable good description of the gas flow [91]. Due to this the 
gas flow in the freeboard is approximated as a plug flow. For BFB there is a high degree of temperature 
uniformity within the bed while the gas temperature decreases within the freeboard due to the endothermic 
gasification reactions taking place. The temperature may decrease as much as 150-200 °C in the freeboard 
depending on the operating conditions and design of the gasifier [92][93]. However, Bridgewater [7] reports 
the temperature drop to only be around 50 °C for typical BFB gasifiers. The assumption about isothermal 
conditions within the freeboard may therefore be unreasonable for some BFB gasifiers but reasonable for 
other gasifiers.  
In order to determine if methyl chloride will appear in the exit gas from CFB gasifiers typically gas residence 
time within the freeboard has to be estimated. In the gasification experiment of [81][90] the gas velocity has 
been reported to be 0.5-0.6 m/s and 0.8-1.4 m/s while the height of the freeboard was 1.25 and 2.5 meters, 
respectively. Thus the gas residence in a BFB is expected to be between 1.8 and 3.1 seconds. The estimated 
residence time is in good agreement with the residence time reported by Corella et al. [94]. They reported, 
the gas residence time in the freeboard of their BFB to be between 2.8 and 3.7 seconds.  
9.3.2 Results  
The thermal conversion of methyl chloride under gasification condition for a BFB has been depicted in 
Figure 50. The rate at which the decomposition of methyl chloride occurs within the BFB is similar to that 
for a CFB. This is not surprising as the conditions in the two gasifiers are similar under the assumptions 
made. However the residence time in a BFB is longer than that of a CFB. Due to this full conversion of 
methyl chloride is obtained for a temperature of 950 °C. For BFB gasifiers operating below 900 °C methyl 
chloride will be present in the exit gas stream. As most of the gasifiers reported in Table 12 operate at 
temperature below 850 °C, it is most likely that a substantial amount of methyl chloride will be present in the 
produced gas from BFB gasifiers. This is supported by Bain and Broer [11] who reports that BFB gasifiers 
usually operates at temperatures between 790 and 870 °C. Furthermore, the temperature may drop 
significantly through the freeboard of a BFB and the thermal conversion of methyl chloride may not occur as 
fast as that indicated in Figure 50. Due to this it seems very reasonable that a substantial amount of methyl 
chloride will be present in the gas produced from BFB gasifiers. 
 
==PAGE:72==
 
Page 70 of 111 
 
 
Figure 50 Thermal conversion of methyl chloride within a BFB gasifier. The gas composition used in the simulation is the 
average value reported in Table 12. 
The pathway for the decomposition of methyl chloride within the BFB is similar to that reported for the 
CFB. Furthermore, the predictions are sensitive to the same reactions and by changing the gas composition 
for the BFB gasifier the same trends as shown in the CFB are observed. Due to this the predictions and 
conclusion made presented above seems reliable. 
9.4 Downdraft  
In this section the conversion of methyl chloride within a downdraft gasifier is modeled. 
9.4.1 Assumptions  
In the downdraft gasifier the pyrolysis and thereby the formation for methyl chloride occurs just above 
combustion zone. Due to this the decomposition of methyl chloride will mainly occur in the combustion 
zone. The decomposition of methyl chloride within the downdraft gasifier will therefore be simulated in the 
combustion zone. The temperature within the combustion zone has been reported to fluctuate between 800-
1200 °C [11] [80] [95] for downdraft gasifiers. Due to this, simulations at different temperatures within this 
range will be performed. 
The gas composition within the combustion zone can be expected to change significantly as partial oxidation 
of the fuel occurs. As no axial gas concentrations have been reported for downdraft gasifiers, the gas 
composition within the combustion zone may be approximated best by the exit gas composition from the 
downdraft gasifier. In Table 13, the exit gas composition for various downdraft biomass gasifiers is reported.  
Table 13:  Exit gas composition from downdraft biomass gasifiers. The gas composition is given on a dry basis in vol%.

From Table 13 it can be seen that oxygen is not present in the exit gas, however oxygen will be present in the 
combustion zone. The oxygen present in the combustion zone may, however react with both the gas phase 
and the solid char. The fraction reacting with the gas phase is unknown. Due to this a worst case scenario in 
which oxygen is absent is simulated. Furthermore, a case where a small amount of oxygen is present will 
also be conducted.  In the worst case scenario the average gas composition reported in Table 13 will be used. 
In the other case a small fraction of the nitrogen will be substituted with oxygen.  
None of the sources in Table 13 have measured the water content of the gas. An estimate of the water content 
in the gas has been made by the gasification group at Risø after personal communication. They estimated 
that the water content will be 26 % on a wet basis. In the simulations performed it is assumed that the water 
content is 26 %.  
In the modelling it is assumed that the gas flow can be approximated by plug flow throughout the downdraft 
gasifier. This has been reported to be a reasonable assumption for fixed bed gasifiers [19]. 
9.4.2 Results  
The thermal conversion of methyl chloride, in the absence of oxygen, is depicted in Figure 51. 
 
Figure 51: Thermal conversion of methyl chloride within a downdraft gasifier. The gas composition used in the simulation is 
the average value reported in Table 13. 
The thermal conversion of methyl chloride occurs very rapidly within the downdraft gasifier, especially 
when the temperature is above 1100°C. At these temperatures the methyl chloride is consumed in less than 
200 ms.  The gas residence time within the combustion zone of a downdraft gasifier is unknown; however, it 
is expected that all methyl chloride will be decomposed as the time for full conversion is so small. To make a 
further justification of this, a simulation where a small amount of oxygen is present is conducted. From the 
experimental data of Zainal et al. [95] the oxygen concentration within the combustion zone was estimated  
to be 11 %. The oxygen within the combustion zone will not only react with the components in the gas phase 
but also the solid char. To make a rather conservative simulation it is assumed that 9 % of the oxygen present 
will react with the gas phase. The predictions for the conversion of the methyl chloride under these 
conditions are depicted in Figure 52.

Based on the predictions, it is highly probable that all methyl chloride is consumed within the combustion

small amounts of oxygen is present. This is expected as the presence of oxygen promotes the formation of 
radicals.

9.4.3 Reaction pathway 
In the presence of oxygen, the reaction pathway for methyl chloride consumption becomes a little more 
complicated than in the absence of oxygen. To make a detailed analysis of the reaction pathway in the 
presence of oxygen, simulations with a few percent of oxygen have been made for all gasification 
technologies. The reaction pathway for the consumption of methyl chloride, in the presence of oxygen, is 
depicted in Figure 54.  
 
Figure 54: Reaction pathway for the methyl chloride consumption under gasification conditions in the presence of oxygen. 
In the presence of oxygen, the majority of the methyl chloride is consumed by the two reactions with atomic 
hydrogen, CH3Cl+H=CH3+HCl and CH3Cl+H=CH2Cl+H2. The fraction of methyl chloride that is consumed 
by these two reactions increases with temperature. However, a substantial amount of the methyl chloride is 
consumed by reactions with other radicals such as OH, CH3 and O, especially at low temperatures. In the 
presence of oxygen, the unimolecular decomposition of methyl chloride does not contribute significantly to 
the consumption of methyl chloride which is contrary to the conditions where oxygen is absent. 
==PAGE:76==
 
Page 74 of 111 
 
The chloromethyl produced by the hydrogen abstraction reactions can be decomposed by several paths. The 
dominating paths are dependent on the temperature. Nevertheless of the pathway, all the chlorine contained 
within the methyl chloride will be converted into hydrogen chloride. At temperatures below 800 °C, the 
reaction with HO2 is the dominating path for the removal of chloromethyl radicals. At higher temperatures 
(>900 °C) the reaction with hydrogen and methyl becomes the dominating path for the removal of 
chloromethyl radicals.  
At low temperatures the chloromethyl is mostly converted into CH2ClO which can eliminate chlorine or 
hydrogen to form formaldehyde and ClCHO respectively. The ClCHO radical is mainly decomposed to 
ClCO by the reaction with methyl; however, the reaction with H2 becomes more substantial as the 
temperature exceeds 1000 °C. The ClCO radical decomposes to CO and Cl. The Cl radical abstracts 
hydrogen from H2 and hydrocarbons and forms hydrogen chloride. The reaction of Cl with CH4 sometimes 
occurs in the reverse direction, the direction of this reaction is depending on the degree of conversion of 
methyl chloride and the temperature. The reaction between chloromethyl radicals and oxygen to form ClO 
does only occur at appreciable rates at low temperatures. The formed ClO may react with CO and H to form 
chlorine or react with formaldehyde and H2 to form HOCl. The HOCl is converted into hydrogen chloride by 
reacting with H. The chlorine containing intermediates (CH2Cl, ClO, CH2ClO etc.) are rapidly consumed 
during the conversion of methyl chloride. When full conversion of methyl chloride is obtained, all the 
chloride containing intermediates have been converted into hydrogen chloride. 
At high temperatures (>900) the majority of the chloromethyl radicals reacts with hydrogen and methyl and 
forms hydrogen chloride. The reaction between chloromethyl radical and methyl also forms chloroethane, 
however it is rapidly decomposed into ethylene and hydrogen chloride under these conditions.  
9.4.4 Sensitivity  
In the presence of oxygen the prediction of methyl chloride becomes sensitive to reactions which consume 
and produce atomic hydrogen as almost all the methyl chloride is consumed by the reactions with atomic 
hydrogen. This can be seen from the sensitivity analysis depicted in Figure 55. 
 
Figure 55: Sensitivity analysis for thermal conversion of methyl chloride within a downdraft gasifier where 1 % of oxygen is 
present in the gas. The residence time was adjusted to achieve 20 % conversion of methyl chloride.

The prediction is very sensitive to the branching reactions H+O2=O+OH as most of the radicals in the system 
is produced from this reaction. This reaction has a negative sensitivity coefficient as the formed O and OH 
radicals produce the majority of the H radicals by reacting with H2. The reactions H+O2(+M)=HO2(+M), 
CH3+H(+M)=CH4+(M) and CH4+H=CH3+H2  have positive sensitivity coefficients as these are terminating 
reactions which consumes hydrogen radicals. The reactions that appear in the sensitivity analysis, Figure 55, 
have all been well characterized and due to this the predictions of methyl chloride seems accurate. 
The prediction of methyl chloride is not very sensitive to the gas composition and due to this the predictions 
and conclusions made seems reliable. This may be seen from Figure 56 and Figure 57.

concentration of each species is increased by 50 % from the average value reported in Table 13. However 0.01 of the mole 
fraction of nitrogen has been substituted with oxygen.
