9.5 Updraft  
For the updraft gasifier it is not necessary to perform simulations. In the updraft gasifier the pyrolysis of the 
biomass, and thereby the formation of methyl chloride, occurs just before the gas leaves the gasifier. The 
pyrolysis occurs at temperatures between 500 and 900 K [96].  As it previously have been seen that methyl 
chloride is not thermally decomposed below 700 °C, it is expected that all the methyl chloride formed in the 
updraft gasifier will appear in the exit gas. Furthermore, it should be noted that oxygen will not be present in 
pyrolysis zone as it is rapidly consumed in the combustion zone at the bottom of the gasifier [96].   
9.6 Entrained bed gasifier  
The entrained bed gasifier operates at high temperatures of 1300-1500 °C [8][11] and with gas residence 
times of a few seconds [97]. In the entrained bed reactor it is assumed that the pyrolysis occurs 
instantaneously when the biomass enters the reactor as it operates at very high temperatures. As it had 
already been demonstrated that the thermal conversion of methyl chloride, in the absence of oxygen, occurs 
very rapidly when the temperature exceeds 1100 °C, it is not necessary to perform simulations for the 
entrained bed gasifier. Furthermore, oxygen will be present in the beginning of the entrained bed gasifiers 
which will further speed up the conversion of methyl chloride. Due to this, it may from the previous 
simulations conducted be concluded that all the methyl chloride that is formed within a entrained bed gasifier 
will be completely decomposed due to the high operating temperature.   
9.7 Injection of secondary air in BFB and CFB 
In fluidized beds it is possibly to introduce a fraction of the total air supply in the freeboard/dilute zone. By 
doing so the majority of the air is introduced at the bottom of the reactor to fluidized the bed, while a small 
fraction of the total air supply may be fed at different levels in the upper section of the gasifier to crack tars 
and improve carbon conversion [85]. When secondary air is injected the total air supply is normally kept 
constant to avoid a further oxidation of the fuel. In other words the equivalence ratio is maintained when 
secondary air is used. It is reported that the use of secondary air only causes slight changes in the gas 
composition when the overall air supply is kept constant [83][93]. Similarly the use of secondary air has 
been reported to only cause a slight change in the heating value of the gas [83][93]. 
The injection of secondary air has been reported only to have a very limited effect on the removal of tar for a 
constant total air supply [83]. However, it may have a significantly effect on the removal of methyl chloride. 
Secondary air may be injected at several axial positions along the gasifier [82] and the optimal location of 
these might be dependent on the design of the gasifier. Generally, it is reported that 10-20% of the total air 
supply is injected as secondary air when secondary air is used [83][93][94]. The mole fraction of oxygen at 
the inlet of the secondary air may be estimated based on the flow rate of gas out of the gasifier and the flow 
rate of air used in the gasifier. Garcia-Ibanez et al. [85] studied a CFB gasifier in which 173 Nm3/h of raw 
gas was produced and 109 Nm3/h of air was used. Gomez-Barea et al. [90] studied a BFB gasifier in which 
31.5 Nm3/h  of raw gas was produced and 19 Nm3/h of air was used. By assuming perfect mixing at the inlet 
of secondary air and constant gas flow through the gasifier the oxygen content at the secondary air inlet can 
be estimated as given in Eq. 9.1. 
 
𝑋𝑂2 =
𝑓𝑙𝑜𝑤 𝑜𝑓 𝑎𝑖𝑟 
𝑓𝑙𝑜𝑤 𝑜𝑓 𝑟𝑎𝑤 𝑔𝑎𝑠⋅𝑋𝑂2,𝑖𝑛 𝑎𝑖𝑟⋅𝑓𝑟𝑎𝑐𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 𝑜𝑓 𝑠𝑒𝑐𝑜𝑛𝑑𝑎𝑟𝑦 𝑎𝑖𝑟 
9.1 
If 10 % of the air supply is injected as secondary air the oxygen concentration can be estimated to be 1.3 % 
and 1.25 % from the two data sets respectively. If 20% of the air is injected as secondary air the oxygen 
==PAGE:79==
 
Page 77 of 111 
 
content is estimated to be 2.5 and 2.6 %. From this it seems seasonable to make simulations in which the 
oxygen content is between 1.25% and 2.5 %.   
When secondary air is injected the temperature slightly increases up through the gasifier [83][94] instead of 
decreasing when only air is injected at the bottom of the gasifier. A temperature profile for a CFB is depicted 
in Figure 58. The temperature increase approximately 50 °C along the height of the CFB gasifier.  
 
Figure 58:Measured temperature profile in the CFB gasifier of Li et al. [83]. 
Similar Corella et al. [94] measured that the temperature difference between the bed and the injection point 
of secondary air to be 50-60 °C for a BFB. Due to this it seems reasonable to model the gasifier as 
isothermal. It should be noted that the use of secondary air may influence the formation of methyl chloride as 
the temperature is lowered in the bed when secondary air is used. It is difficult to assess if more or less 
methyl chloride is formed when secondary air is used. 
In the simulations with secondary air it is assumed that the injection of air occurs at the same position at 
which the methyl chloride starts to decompose. For the BFB it means that the air is injected at the beginning 
of the freeboard and for the CFB it is injected 1.5 meter above the primary air inlet. This assumption seems 
reasonable as the injection of secondary air was located at the beginning of the freeboard in the study of 
Narvaez et al.[93] while the injection of secondary air was located 2.15 meters above the primary air inlet in 
the study of Garcia-Ibanez et al. [85].  
In the CFB the secondary air may not only react with the gaseous species but will also react with the solid 
char in the gasifier. However, here it is assumed that all the oxygen reacts with the gas phase. The 
simulations made in this section will be performed with the same average gas composition as in the previous 
section for BFB and CFB; however some of the nitrogen will be replaced with the estimated oxygen content.  
 
 
==PAGE:80==
 
Page 78 of 111 
 
9.7.1 Circulating fluidized bed 
The decomposition of methyl chloride in a CFB is depicted in Figure 59. 
 
 
Figure 59: Thermal conversion of methyl chloride within a CFB gasifier with 1.5 % (upper) and 2.5 % (lower) of oxygen in 
the average gas composition given in Table 11 
By comparing Figure 59 to Figure 44 it can be seen that secondary air can be used to significantly reduce the 
methyl chloride concentration in the exit gas. At temperatures between 650 and 700 °C, none of the methyl 
chloride was converted in the absence of oxygen while in the present of oxygen a substantially amount of the 
methyl chloride is removed as these temperatures. The presence of oxygen promotes the formation of 
radicals and thereby increases the conversion of methyl chloride.  
At temperatures above 850 °C, the model predicts that it is possible to convert all the methyl chloride even if 
the injection of secondary was located at a higher position in the riser since full conversion is obtained at 
residence times below 0.5 seconds. In the simulations made, the injection of secondary air was assumed to be 
located near the bottom of the CFB and thus a maximum conversion of methyl chloride is obtained. By

9.7.2 Bubbling fluidized bed  
The decomposition of methyl chloride in a BFB is depicted in Figure 60.

750 °C it can be observed that the conversion of methyl chloride almost stops at residence time of three 
seconds. This is because all the oxygen has been consumed at this point and few radicals are therefore

For BFB gasifiers operating at typical conditions it is most likely that all the methyl chloride is consumed

and 870 °C [11] and by injection secondary air the temperature in the freeboard will exceed the temperature 
in the bed and at these temperatures almost all the methyl chloride is consumed.  
9.7.3 Sensitivity 
To identify reactions for which the prediction of methyl chloride is sensitive, a sensitive analyses is 
conducted. The results are depicted in Figure 61. 
 
Figure 61: Sensitivity analysis for conversion of methyl chloride within a CFB gasifier where 2.5 % of oxygen is present in the 
gas. The residence time was adjusted to achieve 20 % conversion of methyl chloride. 
The reactions that appear in the sensitivity analysis are mostly from the methane mechanism. Only four of 
the reactions that appear in the sensitivity analysis are from the chlorinated subset developed in the present 
study. All the reactions that appear in the sensitivity analysis are well characterized, except for the reaction 
CH2Cl+HO2=CH2ClO+OH which the prediction is only slightly sensitive to. Due to this the predictions 
seems trustworthy. 
To investigate how sensitive the thermal conversion of methyl chloride is to the gas composition, simulations 
in which the gas composition is changed are made. The results are depicted in Figure 62.

Figure 62: Conversion of methyl chloride with a CFB at a temperature of 850 °C and with varying gas composition. The 
concentration of each species is increased by 50 % from the average value reported in Table 11.  However 0.025 of the mole 
fraction of nitrogen has been substituted with oxygen. 
The prediction is only slightly sensitive to the gas composition which was also observed previously. The 
same trends as observed previously are also observed here. An increase in the hydrogen content increases the 
decomposition of methyl chloride. An increase in the hydrogen content increases the formation of hydrogen 
which causes the conversion of methyl chloride to increase as the majority of methyl chloride is consumed 
by the reactions with atomic hydrogen. The increase in concentration of methane slows down the 
decomposition of methyl chloride. This might be because the majority of methyl chloride is converted into 
methane and by increasing the methane content of the gas the equilibrium is switched and the conversion is 
slowed down. The prediction is not sensitive to the concentration of the other components. 
In order to investigate how sensitive the thermal conversion of methyl chloride is to the initial concentration 
of methyl chloride chosen, simulations with different initial concentrations of methyl chloride were 
conducted. The results are depicted in Figure 63.

9.8 Discussion  
From the simulations conducted in the previous section, it was found that it is most likely that all the methyl 
chloride formed within the downdraft gasifier and the entrained bed reactor will be decomposed into 
hydrogen chloride due to the high temperatures within these two types of gasifiers. For the updraft and 
fluidized bed gasifier the predictions indicate that a considerable amount of the methyl chloride formed 
within the gasifier will appear in the exit gas. For the updraft gasifier and low temperature fluidized bed all 
the methyl chloride formed within the gasifier will appear in the produced gas, if secondary air is not 
injected. The injection of secondary air in CFB increase the conversion of methyl chloride however it is not 
enough to decompose all the methyl chloride present for gasifiers operating below 850 °C. It is most likely 
that by injecting secondary air in BFB gasifier that all the methyl chloride is consumed within the freeboard 
if the temperature in the freeboard is above 800 °C. 
The concentration of methyl chloride in the exit gas from fluidized bed and updraft gasifiers may be 
significantly lower than the one predicted if the decomposition of methyl chloride is catalyzed by any of the 
solids present in the gasifier. The decomposition of methyl chloride might be catalyzed by inorganic 
materials or char. Due to this it will be useful to measure the concentration of methyl chloride from fluid bed 
gasifiers to verify that methyl chloride will be present in the exit gas from these gasification technologies.  
For the cases in which methyl chloride is present in the produced gas, it has to be removed before the gas can 
be used in turbines, engines or in syntheses. From the prediction of the downdraft gasifier it was shown that 
methyl chloride can be removed selectively if the temperature is high enough. All the methyl chloride may 
be expected to be thermally decomposed within 1 second if the temperature is kept above 1000 °C. The 
product gas from a gasifier may therefore enter a thermal cycle in which the gas is heated to a temperature 
above 1000 °C to reduce the concentration of methyl chloride. However, the use of a thermal cycle may be 
economically unfeasible. Instead of using a thermal cycle, the raw gas from the gasifier may be partially 
0

==PAGE:85==
 
Page 83 of 111 
 
oxidized to remove methyl chloride. The gasification group at Risø has planned to do a partial oxidation of 
the gas produced from their low temperature CFB in order to remove both tar and methyl chloride so the 
product gas can be used in turbine, fuel cells etc. [98]. It is not possible to make a partial oxidation of the gas 
in which methyl chloride is removed selectively as the concentration of methyl chloride in the product gas is 
in the ppm range. Consequently, the heating value of the gas will decrease by making a partial oxidation of 
the gas. However when doing a partial oxidation, the temperature of the gas only has to be raised to 850-950 
°C in order to obtain a full conversion of methyl chloride within a short residence time. The presence of 
oxygen promotes the formation of radicals and therefore the temperature of the gas does not has to be as high  
to obtained full conversion of methyl chloride as when using a thermal cycle.  
Decomposing methyl chloride by either using a thermal cycle or doing a partial oxidation of the produced 
gas does not solve the chlorine problem completely as the methyl chloride is converted into hydrogen 
chloride. The hydrogen chloride has to be removed from the fuel gas before it can be used in turbine, fuel 
cells etc. Hydrogen chloride can however be easily removed by a scrubbing process or adsorption on active 
material as described in section 4. It is fortunate that methyl chloride is fully converted into hydrogen 
chloride doing the thermal conversion and not into other chlorinated species as they might be more difficult 
to remove compared to hydrogen chloride.   
As the  thermal cycle and the partial oxidation converts methyl chloride into hydrogen chloride, which has to 
be subsequently removed, other cleaning methods may be more attractive. By pretreating the biomass, the 
formation of methyl chloride within the gasifier may be avoided. As mentioned in section 2.1.2.1, the 
majority of the methyl chloride is formed from the reaction of pectin and chloride at low temperature. By 
making a thermal pretreatment of the biomass, the majority of the methyl chloride may be released in the 
pretreatment. However some volatiles will also be released during the thermal pretreatment and thus less 
amount of gas will be produced from the gasifier and this will decrease the efficiency of the plant. The 
thermal pre-treatment could be a torrefaction process in which the biomass is upgraded to a high quality fuel. 
Torrefaction is a mild form of pyrolysis and is typically conducted at temperatures from 250-320 °C in an 
inert atmosphere [21]. The gas released from the torrefaction process can be combusted and used to supply 
the energy needed for the torrefaction process. A recent study investigated the release of chlorine from 
different types of biomasses [21]. They found that 20 % of the chlorine in straw was released at a 
temperature of 250 ° and at 350 °C the release of chlorine was increased to 64 %. For woody biomass, they 
found that almost all the chlorine was released at 350 °C. The study found that the majority of the chlorine 
released appeared as methyl chloride. Based on this study a thermal pretreatment of the biomass, may lead to 
a significant reduction in the chlorine content of fuel feed to the gasifier. In addition to the temperature, the 
study found that the relative release of chlorine was dependent on the size of the biomass particles and the 
initial chlorine content of the biomass. The release of chlorine was found to increase when decreasing size of 
the biomass particles while a higher fraction of chlorine was released from biomass with low chlorine 
content.  
In the simulations conducted, the thermal conversion of methyl chloride was predicted in the absence of tar.  
The mechanism developed does only include up to C2 species and therefore it was not possible to do 
simulations where tar is present. In reality tar will be present in the product gas. It is most likely that the 
presence of tar will slow down the thermal conversion of methyl chloride as the formed radicals may be 
consumed by reactions with tar. Furthermore, methyl chloride may react with tar and form other chlorinated 
species. Due to this, the reaction between methyl chloride and tar should therefore be studied in more details.  
==PAGE:86==
 
Page 84 of 111 
 
10 Conclusion  
A chemical kinetic model for thermal conversion of methyl chloride was established. The model was 
evaluated against experimental data from literature on thermal decomposition and oxidation of methyl 
chloride. Overall the established kinetic model gave satisfactory predictions of the ignition delay, burning 
velocity and the product distribution in the flow reactor studies for mixtures containing methyl chloride. 
However, the model under-predicted the ignition delay for methyl chloride mixtures. Furthermore, the model 
slight under-predicted the burning velocity for CH3Cl/CH4/air flames. The model gave very satisfactory 
predictions for the major species in the flow reactor studies. The model performed best under reducing 
conditions which is fortunate as the gasifiers operates under reducing conditions. The kinetic model 
established can be improved by studying the reaction of CH2Cl with O2 and the CH3/CH2Cl and 
CH2Cl/CH2Cl combination reactions in more details as the predictions were found to be sensitive to these 
reactions under certain conditions.   
The kinetic model was found suited to predict the thermal conversion of methyl chloride under gasification 
conditions as it has shown good agreement with the experimental data over a wide range of conditions. The 
kinetic model was used to model the thermal conversion of methyl chloride within different gasification 
technologies (Updraft, downdraft, fluidized bed and entrained bed gasifers). For all types of gasifers the 
methyl chloride was decomposed into hydrogen chloride. For the downdraft and entrained bed gasifier it was 
found that the methyl chloride formed within the gasifier is fully converted into hydrogen chloride and thus 
methyl chloride will not be present in the exit gas form these two gasification technologies. For fluidized bed 
and updraft gasifiers the situation is quite different. In low temperature fluidized bed gasifiers (650-700 °C) 
and updraft gasifiers all the methyl chloride formed within the gasifier will appear in the exit gas. By 
increasing the operating temperature of the fluidized bed gasifier the conversion of methyl chloride within 
the gasifier is increased however a substantial amount of the methyl chloride formed, will still appear in the 
exit gas. The conversion of methyl chloride within the fluidized bed gasifiers can be significantly improved 
by injecting secondary air. By doing so all methyl chloride may be converted into hydrogen chloride if the 
temperature within the CFB and BFB gasifier is above 850 and 800 °C respecitivly. 
In the cases where methyl chloride is present in the exit gas, it has to be removed before the gas can be used 
in the modern energy conversion technologies. From a thermal point of view the methyl chloride may be 
removed selectively by heating the exit gas to above 1000 °C or by making a partial oxidation of the gas 
after the gasification. Decomposing methyl chloride by either using a thermal cycle or doing a partial 
oxidation of the produced gas does not solve the chlorine problem completely as the methyl chloride is 
converted into hydrogen chloride which has to be removed afterwards. Methyl chloride is mainly formed at 
low temperature and a thermal pretreatment of the biomass may be an alternative method for reducing the 
methyl chloride concentration in the gas. Other attractive routes for the removal of methyl chloride could be 
adsorption and catalytic steam reforming however these technologies still remains unproven for this purpose. 
The choice of the cleaning method will be dependent of economics.  
 
 
==PAGE:87==
 
Page 85 of 111 
 
11 Further work 
From the validation of the model it was found that the model predictions were sensitive to the reaction of 
chloromethyl with oxygen, the recombination of chloromethyl radicals and the reactions of chloromethyl 
with methyl radicals. These reactions have never been studied in an isolated environment and have not been 
characterized very well in literature. To improve the mechanism these reactions should be studied in more 
details through experimental studies or at a high level of theory to obtain more reliable rate constants for 
these reactions. The conclusions for thermal conversion of methyl chloride under gasification conditions will 
not be affected by changing the rate constants of these reactions as the prediction under gasification 
conditions is not sensitive to these reactions.  
The model predictions were evaluated against experimental data which was intended for studying the 
kinetics of combustion of chlorinated hydrocarbons. The experimental data used in the present study was 
thus not intended to be used to study the kinetics of methyl chloride under gasification conditions. To verify 
the prediction of the kinetic model established, it will be useful to perform experiments for methyl chloride 
under gasification conditions and evaluate the model predictions against these results. 
From the present study the kinetics on the decomposition of methyl chloride in the gas phase seems to be 
well described. However, little is known on the kinetics for the formation of methyl chloride. The kinetics 
for the formation of methyl chloride should be studied in more details as this might give an insight in how 
the formation of methyl chloride could be limited.   
In the kinetical model established, gas-solids reactions were not taken into account. These might influence 
the decomposition of methyl chloride. Inorganic elements or other solids present in the gasification may act 
catalytically in the decomposition of methyl chloride. Due to this it will be useful to measure the 
concentration of methyl chloride from more fluid bed gasifiers to verify that methyl chloride will appear in 
exit gas from this gasification technology. If, these measurements verify that methyl chloride is present in the 
produced gas, there should be investigated more in the cleaning methods for methyl chloride other than the 
thermal conversion and partial oxidation. Thermal pretreatment of the biomass and catalytic steam reforming 
of methyl chloride could be cleaning methods with high potential.
